Miller v The State of South Australia & Ors [2025] SAET 119 (31 October 2025)
Miller v The State of South Australia & Ors
[2025] SAET

David James
THE STATE OF SOUTH AUSTRALIA
AMACA PTY LTD (FORMERLY JAMES HARDIE & COY

JUDGMENT OF:
His Honour Judge Calligeros
31 October 2025
CATCHWORDS:
Dust Diseases Act
– The applicant has malignant peritoneal mesothelioma and seeks
damages in negligence from the State of South Australia (the
State) for exposure
to asbestos at the Royal Adelaide Hospital (RAH), and from Amaca Pty Ltd (Amaca)
for exposure to asbestos containing
products manufactured by James Hardie and
Coy Pty Ltd (James Hardie) – The State denies liability – Amaca
applicant was exposed to asbestos in its products but asserts the
exposure is much less in terms of quantity and potency compared
to the exposure
to asbestos at the RAH – If found liable, the State seeks contribution
from two third parties (BI) who are
responsible for spraying crocidolite
asbestos on structural steelwork in buildings constructed at the RAH in the
middle of the last
The applicant was exposed to asbestos dust and fibres, predominantly from
crocidolite, when he worked at the RAH in 1991.
2. The applicant was
exposed to asbestos dust and fibres, predominantly from chrysotile, when working
with products manufactured by
James Hardie in employment, self-employment and
performing renovations to a house he previously owned.
applicant relies on the presumptions in
Dust Diseases Act 2005
and has established that the State and Amaca negligently caused him injury.
4. The State owed the applicant a duty of care under s 17c(2) of the
Wrongs Act 1936 (SA) and was in breach of the duty in failing
to provide the
applicant with: safe plant, equipment and supervision; a safe system of work; a
safe place of work; any effective
measures to protect him from asbestos dust and
fibre; effective masks of other respiratory protective equipment; and any
of the danger which he was exposed to.
5. Amaca owed the
applicant a duty of care to warn him of the danger of inhaling, ingesting and
being exposed to dust generated by
using its products and was in breach of that
duty by failing to give the applicant any or any proper warning.
The applicant is entitled to damages in the total amount of
$2,261,927.75.
7. The State is to pay the applicant exemplary damages
of $40,000 pursuant to
Dust Diseases Act 2005
is to pay the applicant exemplary damages of $50,000.
crocidolite asbestos sprayed on structural steel beams at the RAH was sprayed by
the fourth third party and is the responsibility
of the third third
10. The State seeks contribution from BI and is able to rely on
the evidentiary presumptions in
Dust Diseases Act 2005
11. BI knew that the material it sprayed at the RAH was:
injurious to health; incapable of being sprayed accurately and was mis-sprayed
and over-sprayed; likely to become friable and need repair; likely to be
encountered by 7 tradespersons who needed to run services
through or near
12. The State is entitled to contribution equivalent to 40% of the
applicant’s damages from BI. The contribution does not apply
exemplary damages payable by the State.

Applicant: Mr

Miller, the applicant in this proceeding, has malignant peritoneal mesothelioma,
a rare type of cancer. The medical experts
who have treated and assessed Mr
Miller believe that the cancer was caused by asbestos fibres invading his
peritoneum, a thin layer
of tissue that lines the abdominal cavity and protects
the abdominal organs. Mr Miller claims that he was exposed to asbestos when
working as an employee, in operating his own business and in performing
renovations on a former family home.
Miller seeks damages from the State of South Australia (the State) for exposure
to asbestos during a six month period in 1991 when
he worked at the Royal
Adelaide Hospital (RAH) whilst employed by a now defunct labour hire company
(Manpower). Mr Miller also seeks
damages from Amaca Pty Ltd (Amaca), formerly
James Hardie and Coy Pty Ltd (James Hardie), a company that manufactured
products that
contained asbestos.
History of the proceeding
proceeding was commenced on 25 July 2024. A second Statement of Claim was filed
on 15 August 2024.
4 September 2024 the State filed a cross-claim against Amaca seeking
contribution for any liability Amaca might have to Mr Miller.
16 September 2024 Amaca filed a cross-claim seeking contribution from the
party claims brought against two companies Mr Miller performed work for in the
early 1990s, Total Electrical Pty Ltd (Total
Electrical) and Bathroom Concepts
Pty Ltd (Bathroom Concepts) were discontinued. A third party claim brought by
Amaca against BI
has resolved. Third party claims brought by the State against
the third and fourth third parties, Bradford Insulation (Contracting)
(BIC) and Bradford Insulation (SA) Pty Ltd (in liquidation) (BISA) respectively,
remain on foot. The third and fourth third
parties collectively are referred to
as BI in these reasons.
headings used in these reasons are set out below. The non-expert evidence
summaries are lengthy because liability between Mr Miller
and the State was
contested. Fifteen non-expert witnesses gave evidence. The findings I have made
can be understood without reading
the evidence summaries of the non-expert
witnesses, other than Mr Miller’s evidence summary, as the evidence the
findings are
based on is described under the heading: “Findings in
relation to exposure to asbestos at the

Applicant’s non-expert evidence
David Miller
– 19 February 2025
Miller was born on 2 November 1964. He has been in a relationship with his
current partner, Megan England, for 10 years. Previously
he was married and is
the father of two children. Mr Miller has never smoked
cigarettes.
Domestic exposure to
1991 and 2003, Mr Miller and his former wife, Michelle, lived in Brooklyn Park.
The property was a solid brick bungalow home.
In 1994, Mr Miller engaged a
builder to extend the property. The existing ‘lean-to’ clad in
fibro-cement sheets was demolished.
Mr Miller did not demolish the lean-to
himself and was not present when it was demolished. He did perform electrical
work before
the demolition, including removing fittings and switches from what
he believed was asbestos cement sheeting. Doing so created dust
which Mr Miller
got on his hands and
The demolition took a
couple of days. Broken pieces of asbestos cement sheet were left lying around
the back yard. Mr Miller did
not touch the sheets and they were eventually
disposed of by the builder.
Miller also upgraded the switchboard at the property. That involved drilling
through the old switchboard, which he thought was
likely backed with an asbestos
sheet known as Zelemite.
2003, Mr Miller and his former wife moved to a solid brick property in Lockleys
which was built in 1964. Mr Miller lived in the
property for 10 to 11 years. The
only work he did there was to upgrade the lighting. Part of the work involved
drilling a few holes
through the eaves on the exterior of the house to install
outdoor lighting.
He believes the
eaves were lined with asbestos cement sheeting.
Miller is not aware of being exposed to asbestos in any house he has
subsequently resided in, nor is he aware of having any other
exposure to
asbestos generally outside of his working
Exposure to asbestos in employment
Miller left school in 1981 and completed a four-year apprenticeship as an
electrician with Plasteel, a company that constructed
various electrical items
for use by electrical contractors including electrical switchboards for
commercial use.
his apprenticeship, Mr Miller was required to attend TAFE. He was not provided
with any information about asbestos at TAFE
or otherwise during the
apprenticeship.
Plasteel, one of Mr Miller’s jobs was to build electrical switchboards. He
produced about one switchboard per month. The
sheets came pre-cut but he had to
cut ‘U’ shaped slats with a bandsaw and drill holes in the
switchboard. The task took
a few hours to complete but not all of the time was
spent was drilling and cutting. Dust was generated. Mr Miller did not know what
the sheets were made of, but they were black and referred to as Zelemite or
Bakelite. On a couple of occasions, a thinner black sheet
was also used to
install a circuit breaker. The thinner sheets also had to be cut to size with a
bandsaw which created dust.
fuses attached to switchboards contained asbestos cloth to retard fire.
Occasionally the cloth would fall out of place and Mr
Miller would use his
fingers to push it back in. He did not recall that generating
Miller also wired heater boxes. Some of the cabling in the boxes was coated with
asbestos cloth. The coating was a white, woven
material, which left a white
dusty residue on his hands when
completing his apprenticeship Mr Miller took a year off work.
the switchboard division for about a year. He did
not recall using any black
sheeting, Zelemite, Bakelite, asbestos cloth or asbestos coated wires. All the
switchboards he worked
on were metal. The remainder of his time with
O’Donnell Griffin was spent performing electrical work on site. Mr Miller
not aware of having any exposure to asbestos whilst working for
He remained there for two to three years working
on new commercial construction.
He is not aware of having any exposure to asbestos in that
1991, Mr Miller worked as an electrician for Main Industrial for three to four
months. He is not aware of being exposed to asbestos
Manpower and
Miller started working for Manpower in 1991. He was sent to work at the RAH in
mid-1991 and remained there for about six months.
He worked with two witnesses,
Russell Villiers and Dean Bastian, performing similar work to them and working
in the same buildings
The RAH has been demolished
and replaced by another hospital of the same name.
the RAH, Mr Miller performed project-based refurbishment work involving
upgrading electrical equipment and wiring. By reference
to a map of the
he described performing a
lot of work on multiple levels of the East Wing and in service risers as well as
other areas of the map
coloured pink, such as the Theatre Block and the
catacombs were a series of passages in the basement of the RAH where services,
including pipes and electrical cabling ran underneath
the buildings. They were
connected to the service risers and were confined spaces in which it was not
possible to stand up straight.
Mr Miller spent at least a week or two working in
the catacombs over the total time he worked at the RAH. This work usually
pulling electrical cables through the
Miller described the parts of the RAH that he performed work in as very dusty
and dirty. He stated that the RAH was dustier, dirtier
and a worse place to work
in than any other place where he has
perform electrical wiring work, Mr Miller often had to enter ceiling spaces and
service risers. The risers ran from the basement
of buildings all the way to the
top floor and contained electrical equipment and
Miller deposed that it was common knowledge among RAH workers that asbestos was
present in ceiling spaces and service risers. He
learned that information from
talking to other tradesmen who told him they had worked with asbestos for
decades. His knowledge of
asbestos came from co-workers and was general in
nature. His perception was that the risk of being injured by exposure to
was minimal. No mention was made of cancer or death. He did not know
and was not told that much of the asbestos was blue asbestos,
and that exposure
to small quantities of it could cause mesothelioma. He was not told and did not
know that there was no safe level
of exposure to asbestos, nor that taking
the dust home on his clothes could expose his wife and children to illness or
said that had he known these dangers he would not have continued to
work in the presence of asbestos and would have left the RAH
immediately.
Miller deposed that he was never given a written code, procedure or protocol
RAH. He received no training
from Manpower or the RAH about how to work safely with asbestos. He was never
given a mask, respirator
or other personal protective equipment (PPE) to wear at
the RAH and never wore any PPE. He deposed that had he been provided with
he would have used it.
working in ceiling spaces and service risers, Mr Miller saw sprayed asbestos
insulation on steel beams. He stated that it looked
old, was dark blue-grey in
colour and had a rough surface with a flaky and fluffy texture. He also saw blue
material he described
as asbestos in clumps with dust around it not attached to
steel beams. When working in the catacombs underneath the RAH, Mr Miller
that he frequently walked on, disturbed and kicked up clumps of the blue-grey
enter a ceiling space to perform work, Mr Miller had to remove a ceiling tile.
Ceiling tiles rested on aluminium hangers suspended
from the concrete slab above
them. Often the tiles were coated in blue-grey dust, which was disturbed when a
tile was removed. The
dust coated Mr Miller’s face and clothes.
Sometime clumps of material fell down directly onto to his face and clothes when
he was removing a tile. He regularly inhaled the dust created by removing
performing electrical work in a ceiling space, Mr Miller’s head and arms
in were above the level of the ceiling tiles and
in the ceiling space. He could
see what he described as sprayed-on asbestos and fallen clumps of it. He deposed
that his work in
ceilings involved pushing and pulling cables which disturbed
the dust and left it coating his face and
ran up and down the service risers and across the ceiling spaces of buildings.
Service risers were accessed through a door
on each floor leading to a confined
space of about 1 to 1.5 square metres. Mr Miller and other tradesmen constantly
needed to access
those areas. In such areas, Mr Miller could see steel
beams in the ceiling space adjacent to the risers which were almost always
coated in deteriorated dark blue-grey asbestos that was flaky and fluffy.
Equipment and pipes in the service risers were almost always
coated in a dark
blue-grey dust which was easily disturbed. Mr Miller stated that he often had to
brush the dust away before commencing
work. He regularly inhaled the dust when
working in these areas and a residue was left in his hair and on his face and
clothes. He
would sometimes only work in the risers for half an hour, but would
often be in and out of risers all
Miller deposed that he regularly cut and drilled through wall cavities while
completing his work. That generated dust, which landed
on his clothes and face
and which he inhaled as his nose and mouth were within arms’ length of the
source of the dust.
his first affidavit, Mr Miller estimated working in spaces where he saw asbestos
multiple times a week and sometimes several times
a day. He stated that jobs
performed in ceiling spaces could take an hour or
Miller was never provided with a mask or other PPE to prevent inhalation of
dust, nor was he warned about the potential danger
of asbestos dust to his
health while working in these areas. He had heard of a device known as the
bubble that was used to contain
asbestos dust in ceiling spaces, but he never
saw or used one nor was he trained or directed to use one. He did not see PPE
used by workers in the ceiling spaces, service risers or catacombs and no
one at Manpower or the RAH suggested that he should use
Miller deposed that he often saw fluffy clumps of broken off sprayed dark blue
grey asbestos in the catacombs, particularly near
the service risers. He never
saw anyone clean the floor of the catacombs. At times he had to work over clumps
left on the floor,
which he could not help but disturb. He saw it in the air and
became coated in dust on his face, hair and
Miller deposed that the pipes in the catacombs were insulated with lagging,
comprised of a different type of asbestos to that sprayed
on the steel beams.
The lagging was white and looked like Plaster of Paris that had dried hard. It
looked old and parts of it hung
off the pipes or had fallen onto the floor. Mr
Miller stated that he would regularly rub against the pipes when working and
the lagging or kick up dust with his boots. He would regularly inhale
the dust, and it was left in his hair and on his clothes when
he worked in the
Self-employment
Miller said that his dislike of working conditions at the RAH led him to leave
Manpower and start working for himself in or about
of the companies Mr Miller performed work for was Total Electrical. He was
contracted by Total Electrical on and off for a period
of about a year to do
domestic electrical work.
installed residual current devices. The work was mainly performed on houses that
were 15-20 years old or older in the northern
suburbs of metropolitan Adelaide.
He was required to drill through pre-existing switchboards and fibrocement
sheets used to line
eaves. He also drilled through and cut fibrocement sheets
used for wall cladding for some installations, for which he used power
stated that these were dusty jobs which he estimated doing at least weekly, and
sometimes several times a day. He stated
that he regularly inhaled dust when
using power tools to drill and cut. At times the dust fell onto his face as he
worked. The fibro-sheeting
was stamped with the word
Mr Miller did not
recall seeing any warnings about exposure to asbestos or the dangers of asbestos
on the back of Hardiflex sheets.
Miller also performed contract work for Bathroom Concepts on and off for
approximately a year and a half in the early 1990s. He
completed electrical work
for bathroom renovations, mostly on homes, the wet areas of which were usually
timber-framed and lined
with fibro cement sheeting. The fibro sheeting was
branded Versilux.
work Mr Miller did for Bathroom Concepts involved installing electrical cabling
behind wall sheeting in bathrooms before tiling
was done. Most of the houses he
worked in were built in the 1960s to
Bathroom Concepts employee performed demolition work. Mr Miller deposed that he
was often on-site when demolition was taking place
doing electrical work or
waiting to commence doing it. He stated that he was often present when the fibro
sheets were removed. Piles
of broken fibro sheets were left around the site.
Some of them were some stamped Villaboard. At times he had to move or clean up
the broken sheets to perform his work. That generated dust in the air which he
breathed. He had dust on his hands and face after
Miller often saw the back of the fibro sheets that had been removed from
bathrooms and noticed that they had a different texture
to the front. He never
saw a warning on the removed sheets, nor was he told the sheeting was
potentially dangerous by anyone at Bathroom
of sub-contract work for Total Electrical and Bathroom Concepts, Mr Miller
performed some private work, usually domestic electrical
work in houses that
were 10 years old or older. While he had to work in roof spaces with corrugated
fibro sheets known as Deep Six,
he never cut or drilled through them. He
continued to cut and drill through fibro cement sheets lining eaves, on external
and in wet areas, using power tools which generated
He was not aware that the
work posed a risk to his health. He did not realise that fibro cement sheeting
manufactured before the
1980s contained asbestos. He never saw any warnings on
the material itself or in the hardware stores where he purchased the materials,
nor was anything said at any of the building sites he worked on. He deposed that
he would not have done the work in question had
he known the risk to himself and
his family.
the mid-1990s, Mr Miller was performing a lot of retail and construction project
work. In November 1997 he incorporated David Miller
Electrical Pty Ltd, a
company which traded as NGC Electrical (SA) (NGC). Approximately 60% of the work
performed by NGC now is commercial
retail work, including work from well-known
retailers such as Peter Alexander, Smiggle and Nike. NGC also does larger
projects for schools and government work. The business has grown and
now employs 11 tradespeople and hosts 10-15 apprentices at any
Miller has not worked on the tools for NGC in the last 20 years or so. As
managing director his role involves maintaining contact
with clients and
potential clients, providing quotes, arranging the finances and insurance and
organising materials and deliveries.
Mr Miller considers that he generates that
the goodwill of the
Miller is the sole owner of NGC but some of the profits of the business are
distributed to Ms England. Daniel Bourke has worked
with Mr Miller at NGC for
17-18 years and is Mr Miller’s second in charge. Prior to his diagnosis,

Miller attended a GP on 3 May 2024. He was sent for a blood test and scan.
Unhappy with the response, Mr Miller attended the Calvary
Adelaide Hospital
(Calvary) Emergency Department (ED) on 4 May. He underwent a CT scan of his
abdomen and pelvis, which revealed
a large collection of fluid in his abdomen.
He was admitted to hospital and a drain put into his abdomen, through which 28
of fluid was drained within three days. Mr Miller felt better after the
fluid was drained but was
distressed.
Siew Ung Wong, consultant physician, asked Mr Miller about his exposure to dust
at work and said cancer was a possible cause of
the fluid. Mr Miller underwent a
laparotomy on 9 May 2024. He was referred to Dr Nigel Da Silver, surgeon,
who put a camera in his
abdomen and took a biopsy from the lining of his
abdomen. After undergoing another CT scan of his abdomen, Mr Miller was told he
had malignant peritoneal mesothelioma. He and Ms England were shocked and
devastated by the diagnosis. He was discharged on 12 May.
Soon after leaving the
hospital, Mr Miller’s abdomen started to leak fluid, so he returned to the
Calvary ED, was patched up
and sent home
Miller was referred to Dr Chris Lauder, surgeon, at the Queen Elizabeth
Hospital (QEH). Dr Lauder explained the surgery required
involved removing the
lining of Mr Miller’s abdomen and potentially some other organs such as
his spleen and part of his bowel.
He was told that during the operation he would
have hyperthermic intraperitoneal chemotherapy, a warm chemotherapy bath
After being told this was his best chance for survival, Mr Miller

Miller saw Dr Tim Price, oncologist, on 12 June 2024 to confirm he could undergo

admitted to the QEH on 14 June and had another 23.8 litres of fluid drained. Mr
Miller was concerned that
so much fluid had built up so quickly and worried it
would keep happening.
Miller underwent surgery at the QEH on 19 June 2024. In a procedure that went
for more than 12 hours, Mr Miller’s peritoneum
was removed along with his
gall bladder and parts of his omentum and small bowel. He suffered extreme pain,
nausea and breathlessness,
and anxiety in intensive care after the surgery. He
was unable to get out of bed until 22 June and required assistance from two
to do so. On 24 June an epidural was removed. He could then move around
the ward better but was still in great pain, felt nauseous
and had drains in his
abdomen. He had diarrhoea.
the post-operative period Mr Miller felt miserable and could barely eat, sleep
or rest. The drains were removed from his abdomen
on 30 June. He continued to
experience pain in his abdominal area. On 2 July 2024 he was discharged and sent
his abdomen continued to ooze fluid but he was advised that
this was normal.

the time when he started working as an electrician, the tradesmen Mr Miller

The houses looked

fibro cladding, and carpets looked

electrical wiring that Mr Miller replaced in houses was old and damaged,
disintegrating and crumbling. The insulation on the wiring

houses less than 10-15 years
old disintegrate like that. In many of the jobs he
did for Total Electrical and Bathroom Concepts, the earth wire ran loose
the main cable without any insulation, or was missing altogether. The
crumbling wires and lack of earth wire created fire and shock
hazards. The state
of the wiring was like that on almost all the houses he worked on for both
of the wiring was covered in vulcanised Indian rubber (VIR) or had VIR cabling,
that is, a copper conductor insulated with vulcanised
rubber. VIR cables were
usually installed in houses constructed in the 1960s and 1970s or earlier. VIR
was not used in the 1980s
because of the potential for fire and shock hazards as
it ages and disintegrates.
Miller also came across tough rubber sheathed cables known as TRS cables in some
of the houses he worked in. TRS cables were usually
used in houses built up to
the 1970s. The insulation on them began to crumble after about 15-20 years. When
Mr Miller started as
an electrician, TRS cables were contrary to regulations
which required independent and sheathed earth cables, which TRS cables did
have. Mr Miller did not install TRS cables when he worked as an
electrician.
the 1960s and 1970s, thermoplastic sheathed cables known as TPS cables began to
be used. These were sheathed in coloured thermoplastic
or PVC insulation,
similar to what is used today, but not as tough. The original TPS cables began
to crumble after about 20 years,
and did not have an earth wire in them. Most of
the older houses Mr Miller worked on had deteriorating TPS cables. When Mr
started work as an electrician, older TPS cables had to be replaced by a
newer cable with an earth
Miller was also able to estimate the age of the houses he worked on based on

Drury was employed by Manpower as an electrician and worked at the RAH for
Manpower from 1990-95. He was then employed by
the RAH until 2012. Mr Drury did
not know Mr Miller.
Drury was not given any formal induction or training about asbestos at the RAH
and only became aware of its properties by word

idea of the danger of asbestos to his health. He deposed that he did not know
that there was no safe
level of exposure to asbestos when he started working at
Drury became aware there were asbestos zones in the RAH in his early years at
Drury described a device called the bubble which was used to perform ceiling
work in asbestos zones. The bubble had retractable
walls that expanded in the
ceiling space. It was made of steel or aluminium. Its purpose was to create a
space to contain any asbestos
dust that fell from the ceiling. Mr Drury was
given a mask and respirator to wear when he used the bubble but not protective
When Mr Drury started work at the RAH, there was one bubble between
approximately 15 electricians and many other tradesmen, but more
were built during the 2000s.
Drury advised it was not uncommon for him to enter ceiling spaces and find
asbestos present. He found clumps of asbestos in areas
where he understood that
the asbestos had been removed. He stated that records of asbestos locations were
neither comprehensive nor
always accurate.
Drury found barriers separating asbestos areas from non-asbestos zones in
ceiling spaces, however sprayed asbestos was capable
of moving and moved
throughout non-asbestos zones where they were not sealed off.
Drury stated that he was not required to wear a mask or respirator in
non-asbestos zones. He was often surprised by the presence
of asbestos in such
areas. He deposed that sprayed asbestos was present in the north wing, east
wing, the residential building, the
theatre block, outpatients and the services
and teaching block.
was often found in the risers, hollow columns containing services that Mr Drury
often had to work in. The bubble could not
be used in the risers, and Mr Drury
did not wear a mask or respirator in them.
was also found in the catacombs in the basement of the RAH. Pipes with asbestos
were often wrapped in white lagging. Mr Drury
was not required to wear a mask or
respirator when working in the catacombs.
Drury was not informed about blue asbestos. Later he learned that it was
potentially lethal. He learned that from a talk given
by Jack Watkins, a union
member who took an active role in relation to asbestos at the RAH.
Drury deposed that he did not receive a formal induction when he commenced work
at the RAH, nor was he provided with any kind of

relation to asbestos. He was not required to obtain a certificate in relation to
working with asbestos from
any government department before commencing there.
Mr Drury stated that asbestos was removed from the RAH from time to time and
of the companies that removed it was McMahons. If there was a large
redevelopment plan, McMahons completed the removal prior
to work commencing. He
said that asbestos was still being removed when the RAH was demolished.

water to settle any remaining dust, remove their suit

bubble. The operator used a dust monitor in the bubble, but not in the ceiling

a bubble operator had to run a length of cable, they would unzip the bubble, do

Villiers was employed by Manpower as an electrician. He worked at the RAH for a
few months in 1990, before returning in 1991
and remaining there until about
1993. Mr Villiers later commenced in a role with E&BS in 1996, where he
remained until the late
employed by Manpower, Mr Villiers’ work was primarily project-based
refurbishments and renovations. Day-to-day maintenance
was done by RAH

coordinated the work of electricians
and other tradesmen at the RAH.
Villiers worked all over the RAH site including the services and teaching
building, the outpatient block, the theatre block, the
accommodation block and
the north and east wings. To the best of his memory, most of the buildings he
worked in were full of asbestos.
Villiers was not given any formal warning about the danger of asbestos prior to
commencing work at the RAH. He was not given any
safety induction or training
Villiers described a device called the bubble that was used for ceiling work
when asbestos was known to be present. The bubble
was a sealed box on wheels
with a removeable top that pushed up against the ceiling tiles primarily to
prevent any asbestos contamination
from spilling into surrounding areas. Mr
Villiers did not recall being formally trained on how to use the bubble beyond
another electrician
showing him.
Villiers stated there was only one bubble despite there being up to 10
electricians on shift on any given day. The bubble was reserved
for areas known
to have asbestos. Mr Villiers said that he was exposed to asbestos when removing
ceiling tiles in areas where asbestos
was not considered to be present. There
was never a requirement to wear a mask, respirator or use the bubble in such
Villiers stated that he encountered asbestos in service risers. The bubble could
not be used in a riser. He said that in the early
1990s, clumps of asbestos were
present on surfaces in, and the floors of, risers. Nothing was done about that
in his early years
at the RAH. Much later, if asbestos was found in a riser it
would be reported and cleaned up.
was necessary for Mr Villiers to go into the catacombs at least once a month, if

or any protection from asbestos whilst in the catacombs.

Darryl Pitcher
Witness statement
Pitcher was called by the State. He worked as an electrician and completed his
apprenticeship between 1985 and 1989 with FW
Hercus, a machine tools and metal
engineering manufacturer.
December 1989, Mr Pitcher began working at the RAH in E&BS where he remained
until December 2007. Within E&BS, there was
a maintenance division and
projects division, with many of the contractors in the projects division being
employed by Manpower.
RAH maintenance division comprised a maintenance manager who oversaw different
teams, including air conditioning, fitting, electrical,
plumbing and building.
Sub-teams had supervisors who oversaw tradespeople, and team leaders who
reported to the supervisors.
Pitcher was acting team leader of the electrical team within the maintenance
division for approximately 2-3 years before becoming
permanent team leader in
1995. He progressed to being electrical supervisor for 2-3 years, before being
appointed as acting director
of E&BS in July 2005. He remained in that role
until he left the RAH in late 2008.
Reynolds was the Director of E&BS when Pitcher started at the RAH. Pitcher
considered Mr Reynolds to be a great leader around
safety. David McGowan was the
fire and safety officer at the RAH. Mr McGowan’s role also extended
to asbestos safety. Mr McGowan
reported to Mr Reynolds.
Pitcher stated that he was aware of the properties of asbestos before he started
working with asbestos at the RAH. He stated that
in his experience, safety risks
and threats were taken very seriously, and that there was a good culture around
asbestos safety,
with shortcuts not permitted.
Pitcher stated that he understood that there was no safe level of exposure to
asbestos. In his induction at the RAH, Mr Pitcher
said he was provided with
material and information about safety, including the risks and dangers of
asbestos. After his induction,
Mr Pitcher recalled meetings being held in the
morning, known as toolbox meetings, where asbestos was discussed from time to
Because of that, Mr Pitcher said that he was “vigilant when working
with asbestos and t[ook] the necessary
precautions.”
Pitcher understood that the Department of Mines and Energy was involved in
regulating asbestos. He stated that training was provided
by the Department on
the risks and dangers of asbestos. This occurred at the RAH “potentially
once a year,”
he meant it may have been less than
policy on asbestos safety required the use of safety equipment, including
disposable paper coveralls, foot covers and full battery-powered
masks with disposable cartridge filters.
workers shared masks, but later there was an opportunity for workers to order
their own masks. It was up to each individual
to keep their mask clean. Mr
Pitcher’s mask had a battery-powered pump on the front and a disposable
filter. New filters were
always available. Masks were fitted over the top of the
disposable overalls.
a job involved a risk of exposure to asbestos and was likely to take more than
15 minutes to complete, airborne filter monitoring
of asbestos fibres was
ordered. If a job was likely to take less than 15 minutes, PPE was used but
monitoring was not. It was rare
for a job to take less than 15 minutes.
Monitoring was managed by Adelaide Airborne Fibre Monitoring. Mr Pitcher said
that air monitoring
was used in the early 1990s, as he remembers it being used
in an early job completed at the RAH where he was re-installing wiring
4 of the Q wing.
filtered vacuums were used to clean up the worksite and personnel. Disposable
filters, coveralls, overshoes, disposable protective
equipment and contaminated
equipment were disposed of in bags. People would work in twos to ensure that all
the gear fitted correctly
and that each individual was vacuumed off at the end
asbestos register was kept at the RAH. It recorded where asbestos was located.
The Department of Health was responsible for managing
the register. Employees
were to consult Mr Pitcher, who in turn consulted the register before having
them complete a job to determine
whether there was asbestos in the designated
work area. Mr Pitcher said that he used and relied on the register. He also said
there were signs on the ceiling to warn of the presence of asbestos.
Maintenance
staff were involved in auditing and updating the asbestos register. If asbestos
was found and had not previously been
identified on the register, or if it was
removed, a request was made to update the register. Mr Pitcher did not recall
seeing asbestos
in an area where it was not meant to be. He was nevertheless
aware of the procedure if suspected asbestos was found. Workers were
required to
stop what they were doing and report the suspected asbestos to a
supervisor.
Pitcher felt he was “well equipped with knowledge and equipment before
having to work in an asbestos
environment”.
believed that asbestos was taken pretty seriously at the RAH and that there was
never pressure to work in such an environment
or do things in an unsafe

Michael Maybank
Witness statement
– 7 March 2025
Maybank was a refrigeration and air conditioning mechanic by trade and retired
in 2024. He studied at TAFE between 1990 and
1996 and obtained an Associate
Diploma in Mechanical Engineering.
Maybank worked at the RAH between 1980 and 2018. He initially worked as an
apprentice tradesman and transitioned to refrigeration
team leader in the
mid-1980s whilst within E&BS.
Maybank stated that E&BS had a Maintenance division and a Projects division.
The Maintenance division conducted maintenance
work around the RAH and was
comprised of “workshops” including air conditioning and
refrigeration, electricians, fittings,
carpenters, and plumbers. Sometimes
external specialists were engaged when required. The Projects division was for
larger projects,
and at the time, engaged labour hire companies such as Manpower
and other contractors.
1980, when Mr Maybank commenced work at the RAH, he worked in areas where
asbestos was present because it was not prohibited. Mr
Maybank explained
that in around 1982, the Federal government declared that asbestos was a
dangerous substance and gradually types
of asbestos were banned. Mr Maybank was
“hit... hard” when he learnt about the dangers of asbestos given his
the mid-1980s, there were awareness campaigns about the dangers of asbestos led
by unions, such as the one at the RAH led by Jack
the same time, the ALU surveyed the RAH to identify where asbestos was located
and recorded the findings on a register. E&BS
used the register to check for
the asbestos presence of asbestos in working areas and to see if safety
precautions were necessary.
Mr Maybank recalled checking the register.
conducted monitoring and readings of the air to minimise risk to workers. This
was undertaken in and around known asbestos areas.
Maybank recalled that if work was needed in an asbestos area, there were often
alternative means for his team to conduct work,
which involved maintenance and
not installation.
Mr Maybank was in an asbestos area, he used PPE such as overalls, masks and the
bubble. He said that the bubble was a device
that would enable access without
exposing others in the area to asbestos. The bubble was later mechanised, and
occasionally workers
used frames and plastic to create an air lock. Airborne
fibre monitoring was occurring inside and outside the zone, and there was
respirator with a filter mask to put on prior to entering the zone.
exiting the work zone, workers sprayed a mist to settle dust and vacuumed
themselves down. They then de-robed in an air chamber
and confirmed they were
clean before exiting.
Maybank said that by 1991, procedures and safety precautions had been honed. If
a worker encountered asbestos unexpectedly, including
on and in machinery, it
was escalated to a supervisor.
McGowan, the Fire Safety Officer (FSO), provided training which included
respiratory equipment and asbestos training. Mr McGowan
E&BS’ Director. Various other training experts attended the RAH over
the years covering safety around asbestos.
developed its own occupational health and safety committee which reported to
that of the RAH. The committee was heavily involved
with asbestos safety.
Maybank referred to the asbestos registers for the location of asbestos,
however, it was usually everywhere in the ceiling spaces.
He often saw blue
asbestos when lifting tiles, hence the safety precautions and procedures.
Maybank recalls the theatre block was relatively free of asbestos and if there

asbestos removal funding was provided, as it often was for larger projects,

funding was not provided, as was often the case on smaller projects, all normal
working precautions would be applied. This included

Not all smaller projects required workers to be in a
ceiling space.

and create a “working area bubble”
to limit contamination. There
would be a filter to create negative air pressure, such that there would be no

bubble. Monitoring would also occur in the bubble.
were airborne asbestos monitors inside and outside areas where asbestos work was
being conducted, and any removal would require
their use. If a positive reading
occurred, no work could commence until the workers received clearance.

contained no fire load.
1985, a project was put in place to remove asbestos in the RAH. The project
started in 1986 or 1987. Some smaller asbestos removal
had occurred before then
and before Mr McGowan began working at the RAH.
removal of asbestos on a larger scale was underway by 1989. The RAH contracted
asbestos removal jobs out to specialist licenced
asbestos removal companies. Mr
McGowan remembered McMahons was the main asbestos removal company used.
asbestos was removed, everything was sealed off, plastic was everywhere and
people from the removal companies wore full protective
equipment. The specialist
companies put a PVC spray down, scraped the asbestos off and then took the PVC
spray off to catch the fibres.
Removal occurred in the East Wing, North Wing,
Services and Teaching Block and Theatre Block. Mr McGowan cannot remember
when the removal occurred in each area, except in the case of the East
Wing, which occurred in the late 1980s.
asbestos removal occurred, air monitoring companies monitored the rates of

Monitoring and Peter
Seekman. Those companies submitted their results to Mr McGowan and the
Mineral Fibres branch of the DLI. The
asbestos was removed, and air monitoring
had to show safe levels existed before any major works or renovations
McGowan was responsible for the asbestos safety policy at the RAH which was
adopted from the DLI.
employees and contractors were required to use face masks with DLI approved
filters. Mr McGowan clearly recalls people wearing
face masks around asbestos
from when he started working at the RAH.
employees and contractors were required to wear a face mask, gloves, and Tyvek
paper suits/overalls if there was any risk of asbestos
contamination in a work
area. The Tyvek overalls were disposed of under specific conditions. All
employees and contractors had to
use showers after they had been working near
asbestos and in Tyvek overalls.
used, a bubble created a negative pressure and vacuum seal area so that no air
or asbestos fibres could get out of it. Employees
and contractors used the
bubble wearing full protective gear (gloves, masks and Tyvek overalls). All dust
was vacuumed up and water
was sprayed to dampen loose fibres. Air monitoring was
used both inside and outside the bubble when it was used.
was a detailed asbestos register to track where the asbestos was in the RAH. The
register was in the care of the drafting office.
McGowan undertook some of the survey work himself to identify which buildings
had asbestos and was usually accompanied by someone
from the DLI. If asbestos
was found and was not recorded on the register, it was added to the register. Mr
McGowan did that himself,
or the person who found the asbestos would do it. He
is aware that some people from air monitoring companies added to the register.
When new entries were made on the register, he had to inform Glen Reynolds.
any work commenced, both employees and contractors were required to consult the
asbestos register to determine whether asbestos
was in the intended worksite. If
asbestos was recorded on the register, full personal protective equipment was
required, regardless
of whether the bubble was used. If large scale work was to
occur, asbestos was removed by the removal companies before the work
McGowan frequently checked on employees and contractors when they were working
to ensure that appropriate fire and safety precautions
were undertaken. This
included asbestos safety precautions.
McGowan organised annual asbestos training at the RAH, which began before his
coronary bypass at the end of 1989. During the training
he explained “the
dangers of asbestos, how it sticks to your lungs and stays forever, and how you
needed to protect your
breathing”.
demonstration, he also taught practical skills like how to properly fit a mask
and overalls.
Contractors
who were to undertake work around asbestos, including electrical contractors,
were required to have an asbestos certification
from the DLI. This certified
that the contractor had completed asbestos training. Some contractors, such as
builders and bricklayers,
did not require these certificates because they were
undertaking new building work and not working near asbestos. Mr McGowan sighted
and checked the certificates of the contractors.
the mid to late 1980s, there was a big union presence at the RAH. If there was
any suggestion of asbestos being found, unions campaigned
and applied pressure
to shut down the site. Mr McGowan was vigilant because of that.
was responsible for ensuring that its contractors were properly trained and able

Supplementary witness statement – 17 June 2025
McGowan was employed at the RAH when ceiling spaces were not properly sealed to
prevent fire spreading. They rectified this by
installing fire barriers in the
ceiling spaces to correspond with fire doors on the floors below. Fire barriers
were comprised of
plaster board.
the mid 1990s, the fire separation system had been installed in the North Wing,
East Wing, Residential Block and the three main
buildings being the Theatre
Block, Outpatients Block and the Services and Teaching Block. The Residential
Block was the last area
to be installed.
ceiling spaces in the buildings were interconnected with another building other
than the Theatre Block, Outpatients Block and the
Services and Teaching Block at
ground level and first floor level.
McGowan introduced fire barriers in the service risers where plaster board was
installed at each floor level in each building.
This blocked off the service
riser at floor level to prevent the spreading of fire vertically. Holes were
created for the pipes to
travel which were encased in a fire pillow. This was
also the case for cables.
asbestos was found in a location where it was not meant to be, the procedure was
to immediately close the riser door. Riser doors
were locked and a key was
required to access them.
asbestos was encountered in a ceiling space, the procedure was to replace the
ceiling tile and report the asbestos. Work was not
allowed to continue under any
circumstances.
McGowan recalled arranging for suspected asbestos to be tested. On most
occasions, it was not asbestos. Only once or twice was
it asbestos. It was found
on a fire barrier in a riser, and it looked like fluff.

Re-examination
air flow in the risers, Mr McGowan described a phenomenon known as the stack
effect by which, he said, air in the building
warms up along with air in the
risers. It occurred because the ambient temperature in a building is higher than
the temperature outside,
except on a hot day. He said that an updraught in
risers keeps asbestos fibres suspended at the top of the risers
indefinitely.

turning to the evidence of the Manpower electricians, it should be noted that
community attitudes towards health risks were
very different in 1991 than they
are today. The risks and consequences of cigarette smoking were known by 1991.
The National Tobacco
Campaign did not commence until 1997 and smoking was not
banned in restaurants, hotels and coffee shops until 2007. Smoking was still
allowed in public areas of the South Australian Industrial Relations Court in
the late 1980s.

the end of re-examination, Mr McGowan described a phenomenon known as the stack
effect by which, he said, air in the building warms
up, as does air in the
risers. He said that there was an updraught at all times and suggested that
asbestos fibres would remain suspended
at the top of the updraught in risers
indefinitely. He implied that the stack effect meant asbestos particles remained
do not accept Mr McGowan’s description of the stack effect. It was not
established that he has any expertise in relation to
the subject and his
assumption that the temperature in risers is always higher than the outside
temperature does not account for
draughts that may have occurred when doors to

Reynolds was ultimately responsible for all works in the RAH and “also had

delegated responsibility for

David [McGowan] was delegated to be the person who was responsible for asbestos
safety in the hospital and this responsibility filtered
down to the respective
divisional managers, section leaders and trade supervisors.
Reynolds’ comment that asbestos safety ‘filtered down’ to
other managers must be construed in the knowledge that
neither he nor

tradespeople at the RAH, considered they had any
responsibility for providing
training about asbestos to external contractors like Mr Miller.
find that there was a significant lacuna or void with asbestos safety and
training at the RAH in 1991. Any training, and it would
appear any proper
supervision, was directed to RAH/E&BS employees who primarily worked in the
maintenance division. No training
was given to external contractors. Based on
the evidence of Mr McGowan, I reject the evidence of any witness who asserted
Miller was or would have been provided with an induction and training in
relation to asbestos.
Reynolds stated that inductions “included all contract workers as
I take that

Manpower electricians all said that they encountered asbestos in the early part
of their time at the RAH. That is not surprising.
As Mr Villiers said, the RAH

“sprayed asbestos

Precautions
such as wearing PPE and using the bubble may have applied to RAH/E&BS

find that Mr Miller and the Manpower electricians were not given a mask, not

always wear a mask when in

Mr Miller stated in his first affidavit that he encountered asbestos more
frequently than the Manpower electricians stated they
did, I am satisfied that
he worked in proximity to crocidolite and inhaled fibres. I am satisfied that
his overstatement was due
to his incomplete memory and not deliberate or
designed to support his claim. As will be seen, the evidence of Professor Klebe,
I accept, was that a single, one hour exposure to crocidolite is capable
of causing mesothelioma.
Crocidolite
was used to fire-proof structural steel in RAH buildings constructed between the
late 1950s and the early 1970s. Crocidolite
is friable. As it ages, it becomes
brittle. Sprayed crocidolite can and did fall off surfaces in clumps as the
photos in exhibits
demonstrate.
The exhibits
contain photos of blue, white and grey coloured asbestos in various locations in
the RAH. The photos are in a document
created by the RAH in 1994 and/or
Based on expert
evidence described later in these reasons, if a clump of crocidolite is
disturbed by being kicked on the floor of
the catacombs or moved when lifting a
ceiling tile, asbestos fibres become airborne, move in the air and can be
inhaled. Mr Hawkins
gave evidence to that effect. He was a member of the AMU.
His evidence supports the evidence of the Manpower electricians that crocidolite
moved from the beams it was sprayed onto. To the extent that other RAH witnesses
said that crocidolite could not make its way into
risers, ceiling spaces or the
catacombs, I reject that evidence.
evidence of most of the non-expert witnesses called by the State did not
directly engage with or contradict the evidence of Mr
Miller and the Manpower
electricians. The State’s witnesses generally worked under very different
conditions to Mr Miller and
the Manpower electricians. The reasons which follow
explain the differences, and explain why I prefer the evidence of Mr Miller
the Manpower electricians to the evidence of State’s witnesses where
there is conflict between them.

Miller’s closing submissions provide the following context in relation to
Amaca was formerly known as James Hardie & Coy Pty Limited. From about 1937
to about 1987 it was the largest manufacturer and
distributor of asbestos cement
building materials in Australia. From 1977, it was the sole manufacturer and
distributor of asbestos
cement building materials in Australia. Until 2001,
Amaca was a wholly owned subsidiary of James Hardie Industries Limited
its parent company.
Amaca manufactured and sold a variety of asbestos cement building materials
including flat and corrugated sheets and building planks
which were used to
build commercial, industrial and residential buildings. James Hardie has been
responsible for 70% of Australian
consumption,
proportion of this being used by Amaca in the production of building materials.
Amaca’s asbestos cement building materials
continue to form part of
hundreds of thousands of buildings throughout Australia including houses. In the
1980s Amaca considered
that there were “
asbestos building products, in
some form, in almost every building in
majority of houses throughout Australia built prior to 1985 had Amaca’s
building product in the eaves and wet
Importantly, from 1 January 1961 Amaca had a monopoly on the production and sale
of asbestos cement building materials in the State
The evidence of
its long term employee, Mr Graham Allen, confirms that it was the sole
manufacturer of asbestos cement fibro products
in South Australia and that
Amaca’s asbestos cement products were the only type of fibro products sold
in South Australia after

continued to operate in South Australia as a distributor of products

Expert evidence in relation to foreseeability of risk of injury
Michael Kottek
Kottek is an occupational and environmental consultant/industrial hygienist. He
holds a Bachelor of Science degree with Honours
and has conducted literature
surveys and retrospective exposure and risk assessments for a variety of
chemical and physical hazards.
Mr Kottek has given evidence in courts and
tribunals across Australia in relation to asbestos. He provided an expert report
Miller’s solicitors after being provided with Mr Miller’s
history of exposure to
He was asked about

Kottek stated that the occupational exposure limits that applied during Mr
Miller’s work were those from the NHMRC, and later
the National
Occupational Health & Safety Commission (NOHSC) Safe Work Australia exposure
The limits were
adopted from the British Occupational Hygiene Society (BOHS) standards in 1969,
which at the time expressed that
100 fibre millilitres per year (equivalent to 2
fibre mls per year for 50 years) would result in a one per cent chance of
asbestosis. The BOHS standards were not intended to directly protect
against any disease other than asbestosis, so they did not apply
mesothelioma.
1969 exposure standards are referred to on page 3, para. [3] of
Mr Kottek’s report. Upon reflection, he said that by around
NHMRC had adopted a 0.1 fibre mil exposure standard for crocidolite, and in 1984
adopted the same level for amosite. This
was an evolution of the 1969 standards
and their intended purpose. These changes did not have an epidemiological goal
of limiting
asbestosis to a particular level but were rather aimed at reducing
exposure to these materials.
was given about further changes to exposure standards. The 1991 Code of Practice
adopted an exposure standard of 0.1 fibre
Kottek noted that prior to 1991, there had been a general belief that exposure
to asbestos should always be minimised. Even at
that time, exposing someone to
0.1 fibre mls of crocidolite for eight hours a day, 40 days a week would have
been considered “an
astonishing burden of mesothelioma”. Reliance on
numeric exposure standards was not considered as “the be-all and
reduction in South Australia to a standard of 0.05 fibre mls was to highlight
where there should be control. Mr Kottek noted that
the control limits are
usually over a much shorter period of time, and if properly implemented, would
act to bring the long-term
average exposures well below the exposure

well as its continued use, another reason for the differing exposure standards

Re-examination
Kottek agreed that if Mr Miller’s self-employment as an electrician

Kottek said that the first NHMRC standards were first published possibly as
early as 1945. The NHMRC continued to publish standards
from time to time until
the NOHSC replaced the NHMRC. Since about 1960, the standards were published
with a proviso highlighting
a limitation in preventing mesothelioma and cancer
risk, noting that individual susceptibility may be a relevant factor in relation
to risk and stating that there is no safe level of exposure to
Earlier versions of
the standards contained general warnings relating to asbestos, but not specific
information like this.
Kottek explained that personal asbestos monitoring involves measuring the level
of asbestos in an individual worker’s breathing
zone and their personal
exposure. Static monitoring involves measuring the exposure levels in the
workspace around where someone
is working. He said that when friable asbestos is
removed, static monitoring is usually used and the exposure standard that is
to is usually a very low level, to ensure low exposure in the workspace
and also to minimise leakage from these areas to outside
areas. It is possible
for a static monitor to not record a breach of the exposure standard while a
personal monitor will do so.
Relevant provisions of the Dust Diseases Act/Regulations
8(1) and (2) of the DD Act provide:
(1) If it is established in a dust disease action that a person (the
injured person
(a) suffers or suffered from a dust disease; and
(b) was exposed to asbestos dust in circumstances in which the exposure might
have caused or contributed to the disease,
it will be presumed, in the absence of proof to the contrary, that the exposure
to asbestos dust caused or contributed to the injured
person's dust disease.
(2) A person who, at a particular time, carried on a prescribed industrial or
commercial process that could have resulted in the
exposure of another to
asbestos dust will be presumed, in the absence of proof to the contrary, to have
known at the relevant time
that exposure to asbestos dust could result in a dust
Miller submitted that the State and Amaca come within s 8(2).
Schedule 1 of the
Dust Diseases Regulations 2021
“[m]anufacture of products containing asbestos” is a prescribed
industrial or commercial
“installation of products containing asbestos (whether in buildings, plant
and equipment, vehicles or vessels or
otherwise)”.
electrical work performed by Mr Miller at the RAH also falls within this
prescribed industrial or commercial
Any industrial or commercial process involving asbestos or products containing
asbestos (not being a process referred to in items
1 to 9 above, and not
including where the only involvement of asbestos or products containing asbestos
in the process is the presence
of asbestos or the product in a structure
comprising or forming part of the premises in which the process occurred)
my view, Mr Miller was carrying out a prescribed industrial or commercial
process within the meaning of s 8(2) of the DD Act when
he was exposed to
asbestos whilst working at the RAH and with James Hardie products.

Miller cited the following passage, which usefully summarises the evolution of
knowledge of asbestos in Australia, in
Bendix Mintex Pty Ltd v
Asbestos has been a well known hazard in industry for at least 60 years.
Numerous studies and papers have been published from the
1920s onwards in
respect of its dangers. Many of the studies have been in the context of dust
hazards generally and some were specifically
directed to asbestos. Intermittent
exposure to asbestos was considered a problem as early as 1950 when Dr Smith, a
respected expert
in the field, reported:
[Under] “conditions of intermittent exposure it is more difficult to
predict the likely effect on the lungs than if the exposure
was continuous but
the severity of any dust hazard is usually less in such circumstances. This
might, however, not apply with high
intermittent exposures.”
In summary, therefore, the knowledge available in the field of industrial health
at least by 1960 was that the inhalation of asbestos
was dangerous. It had been
known for many years that it could cause asbestosis and by 1960 that it could
cause mesothelioma. At that
stage no particular distinction was drawn as to
whether asbestos in all its forms was dangerous or whether only particular types
of fibre were dangerous. The studies up to that time, at least for the most part
had involved crocidolite. However, during the 1960s
a distinction between fibres
was beginning to be drawn. Likewise studies from about this time indicated that
end users of asbestos
products could be exposed to inhalation of raw fibre and
that chrysotile could cause asbestos related diseases.
Whilst these concessions did not specifically relate to chrysotile, it was not
necessary, in my opinion, for the respondent to establish
that it was
foreseeable that exposure to a particular type of asbestos could cause injury.
At the relevant time it was known that
asbestos, as a generic substance, was
Julia Farr Services v
the NSW Court of
Appeal said:
In my opinion, they (
Bendix Mintex v Barnes
CSR v Young)
fatal to the appellant’s submissions. Foreseeability can turn on knowledge
of the dangers of exposure to asbestos, including
in the calculus for breach of
duty, and is not confined to knowledge of the dangers of exposure to high levels
of asbestos or levels
of asbestos above any particular standard.
Duty of care – first respondent

Miller submitted that as the owner and occupier of the RAH, the State owed him a

or minimising foreseeable
risk of injury:
Australian Safeway Stores Pty Ltd
referred to the
Civil Liability Act 1936
(CLA) s 20(1) of which provides:
Subject to this Part, the liability of the occupier of premises for injury,
damage or loss attributable to the dangerous state or
condition of the premises
shall be determined in accordance with the principles of the law of negligence.
Miller submitted there is no difference between the law applicable to an
occupier’s liability for an injury attributable
to the dangerous state or
condition of the premises by virtue of s 20(1) of the CLA, and the law
applicable to the occupiers liability
for injury caused in any other way under
general negligence principles. An exception to that statement was said to be the
be considered to consider the statutory factors in determining the
standard of care (breach):
Miller relied on the principles enunciated in
Stevens v Brodribb Sawmilling Co Pty
He submitted
...the High Court recognised that, depending on the circumstances, a
non-employer/occupier who engages an independent contractor
may be personally

based upon notions of proximity and

that proximity as a test has been replaced, Stevens remains good law on the
of the reasonable response of an occupier who, as the State did at the

and implementing
the system of work (including with respect to independent contractors) on

State disagreed and submitted that ordinary common law occupiers’

accept the State’s submission. Section 17C of the Wrongs Act, formerly s

RAH. Section 5 of the
Law Reform (Ipp Recommendations) Act 2004
Recommendations Act) redesignated the Wrongs Act as the CLA and s 15

Relevantly to this
proceeding, sch 1 of the Ipp Recommendations Act
contains the following
transitional provision:
1— Transitional provision
(1) The amendments made by the
Law Reform (Ipp Recommendations) Act 2004
Ipp Recommendations Act
) are intended to apply only prospectively.
(2) If a cause of action that is based wholly or partly on an event that
occurred before the commencement of the Ipp Recommendations
Act arises after the
commencement of the Ipp Recommendations Act, it will be determined as if the
amendments had not been enacted.
Suppose that A was exposed to asbestos in 1990 but a resultant illness is not
diagnosed until after the commencement of the Ipp Recommendations
Act. An action
is then brought in negligence in which damages are claimed for personal injury.
The amendments made by the Ipp Recommendations
Act would not affect the
determination of liability or the assessment of damages.
1(2) of sch 1 of the Ipp Recommendations Act makes it clear that redesignating s
17C of the Wrongs Act as s 20 of the CLA does
not affect the statutory basis of
this proceeding.

Wrongs Act 1936
to this matter,
as in effect from 31 October 1991
to 2 May 1993, provided:
OCCUPIERS LIABILITY
Interpretation
In this Part, unless the contrary intention appears–
"dangerous" includes unsafe:
"landlord" includes a landlord under a statutory tenancy:
"occupier" of premises means a person in occupation or control of the premises,
and includes a landlord:
"premises" means–
a building or structure (including a moveable building or
structure);
a vehicle (including an aircraft or a ship, boat or vessel).
Occupier's duty of care
(1) Subject to this Part, the liability of the occupier of premises
for injury, damage or loss attributable to the dangerous state
or condition of
the premises shall be determined in accordance with the principles of the law of
negligence.
(2) In determining the standard of care to be exercised by the occupier of
premises, a court shall take into account–
the nature and extent of the premises;
the nature and extent of the danger arising from the state or
condition of the premises;
the circumstances in which the person alleged to have suffered
injury, damage or loss, or the property of that person, became exposed
the age of the person alleged to have suffered injury, damage or
loss, and the ability of that person to appreciate the danger;
the extent (if at all) to which the occupier was aware, or
ought to have been aware, of–
(i) the danger;
(ii) the entry of persons onto the premises;
the measures (if any) taken to eliminate, reduce or warn against the
the extent (if at all) to which it would have been reasonable and
practicable for the occupier to take measures to eliminate, reduce
against the danger;
any other matter that the court thinks relevant.
The fact that an occupier has not taken any measures to eliminate,
reduce or warn against a danger arising from the state or condition
of premises
does not necessarily show that the occupier has failed to exercise a reasonable
standard of care.
(4) Subject to any Act or law to the contrary, an occupier's duty of care may be
reduced or excluded by contract but no contractual
reduction or exclusion of the
duty affects the rights of any person who is a stranger to the contract.
(5) Where an occupier is, by contract or by reason of some other Act or law,
subject to a higher standard of care than would be applicable
apart from this
subsection, the question of whether the occupier is liable for injury, damage or
loss shall be determined by reference
to that higher standard of care.
Miller and the State agree that the 1987 amendments to the
effectively adopted the High Court’s approach to occupiers liability in
. In a 2000 decision, Doyle CJ observed that in the second reading
speech the Minister referred to doing “away with the outmoded
by reference to which the liability of occupiers has been
determined”.
Miller and the State both referred to
a case in which
the High Court considered
. Passages from the judgments
of Gleeson CJ and Kirby and Hayne JJ were cited. Gleeson CJ observed that paras
(a)-(g) of s 17C(2)
included factors “that, in one way or another, were
taken into account in the old common law categories, but the inflexibility
the old approach was not
Although in
dissent about the outcome, Kirby J agreed that the common law standards were
substantially retained and emphasised that
it was essential for courts to take
into account the matters referred to in s 17C(2) and
Hayne J agreed with that
and said this:
Account must be taken of each of the matters that is identified. But in doing so
it is important to recall that s 17C(3) makes plain
that there are cases in
which doing nothing to eliminate, reduce or warn against a danger is consistent
with exercising reasonable
care. In particular, demonstrating that an occupier
"was aware, or ought to have been aware of" both "the danger" that led to an
entrant being injured and "the entry of persons onto the premises" does not
require the conclusion that the occupier should have
taken some step to
eliminate, reduce or warn against that danger. Rather, it is necessary to take
of the matters specified in s 17C(2), including
"the extent (

the Full Court said the
Insofar as the defendant's liability to the plaintiff arises as an occupier of
operates as a code which displaces the
common law in the absence of intentional injury damage or loss as mentioned in
s17E(2) abovementioned.
There is no allegation in the present case which would
bring s17E(2) into operation. Therefore, applying s17E(1) I conclude that
common law has no direct application to this case although its principles
generally remain by virtue of s17C(1) of the
which reads as
“Subject to this Part, the liability of the occupier of premises for

or condition of the

Miller and the State each made reference to the following passage in the
judgment of Mason J in
Wyong Shire Council v
In deciding whether there has been a breach of the duty of care the tribunal of
fact must first ask itself whether a reasonable man
in the defendant's position
would have foreseen that his conduct involved a risk of injury to the plaintiff
or to a class of persons
including the plaintiff. If the answer be in the
affirmative, it is then for the tribunal of fact to determine what a reasonable
man would do by way of response to the risk. The perception of the reasonable
man's response calls for a consideration of the magnitude
of the risk and the
degree of the probability of its occurrence, along with the expense, difficulty
and inconvenience of taking alleviating
action and any other conflicting
responsibilities which the defendant may have. It is only when these matters are
balanced out that
the tribunal of fact can confidently assert what is the
standard of response to be ascribed to the reasonable man placed in the
defendant's
The considerations to which I have referred indicate that a risk of injury which
is remote in the sense that it is extremely unlikely
to occur may nevertheless
constitute a foreseeable risk. A risk which is not far-fetched or fanciful is
real and therefore foreseeable.
But, as we have seen, the existence of a
foreseeable risk of injury does not in itself dispose of the question of breach
The magnitude of the risk and its degree of probability remain to be
considered with other relevant factors.
authority referred to by the State was
Scaffold Services Pty Ltd v Adelaide Brighton Cement
where Doyle CJ
I approach these issues on the basis that ABC was the occupier of the premises
generally, putting aside for the moment the question
of who was the occupier of
the chute. In my opinion the Judge was right to conclude that ABC took
reasonable care to ensure that
workers on the site were informed about safety
issues generally, in relation to safety at its premises in particular and the
was right to conclude that ABC took reasonable care to control entry into
confined spaces. There can be no doubt that ABC employed
competent contractors
to carry out the maintenance work and to provide scaffold. I proceed on the
basis that Mr Henry was injured
because of the failure by Scaffolding, a
specialist contractor, to carry out its work carefully and safely.
common law duty of care as an occupier
As an occupier of the premises in question, ABC owed a duty
to take reasonable care for the safety of persons entering the premises,
including persons like Mr Henry:
(SA) s 17C. That duty would include
a duty to take reasonable care to protect a person like Mr Henry from harm
resulting from the
activities of contractors working on ABC's premises. That
duty in turn could involve a duty to warn an entrant to the premises about
source of harm, or a duty to keep an entrant away from an area in which
contractors are working. Subject to that, the main focus
of the duty of an
occupier relates to the state of the premises, rather than to activities taking
place on the premises. The duty
of ABC as occupier of the premises would not
usually extend to a duty to protect a person on the premises, not being an
of ABC but an employee of a contractor working on the premises, from
harm that resulted from the carelessness of the worker's own
employer, or from
harm that resulted from the manner in which another contractor carried out its
work. Once again, even that proposition
has to be qualified. As occupier of the
premises ABC had a duty to consider safety issues arising from the presence of
Scaffolding on ABC's premises, and from the interaction between those
two firms as contractors separately engaged for the one task.
In my view, to the extent that Mr Henry makes a claim
against ABC as occupier of the premises, that claim is answered by the
that as occupier ABC was not under a duty to supervise the manner in
which workers employed by Scaffolding went about the task of
scaffolding. And it was the manner in which they did that that led to Mr Henry's
injury. Putting it a little differently,
ABC as occupier was under no duty to
protect Mr Henry against the risk of harm as a result of workers employed by
Scaffolding erecting
a scaffold that was not safe. In my opinion, this aspect of
the claim by Mr Henry against ABC fails.
the State relies on the last paragraph in that passage, Doyle CJ found that the
duty of care owed by an occupier under s 17C
duty to take reasonable care of persons entering the premises. In my view, that
duty includes advising external contractors
that they are working in the
presence of a toxic and potentially lethal material.

The measures (if any) taken to eliminate, reduce or warn against the
State took measures in relation to working with asbestos in designated asbestos

extent to which it would have been reasonable and practicable for the occupier

State submitted that there is a difference between what is practicable and what
is reasonable and the latter term means what could
have been done. The first
definition of ‘practicable’ in the Macquarie Dictionary
capable of being put into practice, done, or effected, especially with the
available means or with reason or prudence; feasible.
word ‘reasonable’ has a broad and protean meaning in law,
particularly in relation to the law of negligence. I take
“reasonable and
practicable” in the present context to mean measures that were appropriate
to the degree of risk and
capable of practical implementation. I accept the
State’s submission that the standard that applies is that of 1991.
most obvious reasonable and practicable measure that could have been taken was
to warn and train external contractors like Mr
Miller about the presence and
danger of asbestos dust in the RAH. The failure to warn and train all
tradespersons who performed work
at the RAH in relation to asbestos was an
obvious oversight that could easily have been remedied.
authorities about warnings relied upon by the State do not cause me to vary that
view. The presence of a large amount of crocidolite
in a public hospital that
thousands of people attended weekly warranted a warning. The RAH acknowledged
that by warning its employees
and the contractors that it engaged directly.
Papatonankis v Australian Telecommunications
Commission and Another
is authority for the proposition that an occupier is not under a duty to
give a warning about a defect in premises if tradesmen are
“accustomed to
meeting and safeguarding themselves against defects of that
The presence of
asbestos in a workplace in 1991 was not a matter that Mr Miller or any of the
Manpower electricians were accustomed
to dealing with. While training is
ordinarily an employer’s
obligation,
found that the State has not established that Manpower was advised of the
presence and prevalence of asbestos in the RAH.
For that reason, cases where the
employer is found to owe a duty to warn rather than the occupier do not assist
this analysis. That
factor distinguishes this case from the comments Doyle CJ

that the State relies upon. The State
has not established that the RAH took any steps to ensure that external
contractors were informed

Any other matter that the court thinks relevant
State submitted that training does not fall within the scope of an
occupier’s standard of care and is not found in the wording
of s 17c(2).
While placita (f) and (g) of s 17c(2) do not mention training, training was an
effective, reasonable and practicable
measure that could have been taken to
“reduce or warn against the danger” in my view. Proper training is a
fundamental
plank of a proper approach to workplace safety.
(h) of s 17c(2) allows a court to take into account “any other matter [it]
thinks relevant”. Providing a warning
to someone who may encounter a
carcinogenic substance is a relevant matter in my view. The RAH warned its own
employees and the contractors
it engaged directly about the presence and danger
of asbestos. In the absence of proof that Manpower was advised about the
and danger of asbestos dust at the RAH, the RAH and the State were
responsible for providing that advice.
State’s written submissions described Technical Bulletin
Technical Bulletin 24 was released by the SAHC some time prior to 30 June 1980.
It was a comprehensive document addressing a number
of issues, including the
potential hazards posed by exposure to asbestos, identification, legislation,
responsibility for the control
of hazards, procedures during minor works in
ceiling spaces, procedures during major alterations, procedures in rooms
exposed sprayed asbestos, respiratory protection, protective
clothing, the use of vacuum cleaners and waste disposal. The purpose
Technical Bulletin 24 was ‘
to lay down procedures to be adopted by
those engaged in maintenance and alteration work in the vicinity of sprayed
asbestos coatings.
’ Among other things, it specified that exposure to
asbestos should be kept as low as possible.

Full Court in
said the following about the duty of care owed by
James Hardie:
It is a necessary, but not sufficient condition, for the imposition of a duty of
care in the use of a product, that the manufacturer
or distributor ought to have
foreseen that risk. In this case Mr Werfel must prove that James
Hardie should have foreseen a material
risk that persons engaged only
occasionally in working on in‑place asbestos home building products, as
tradespeople or householders,
could contract mesothelioma. The other
circumstances supporting the imposition of a duty to warn, or negating it, are
to be found
in the matrix of circumstances connecting the manufacturer or
distributor with, or distancing it from, the person who suffers a foreseeable
injury. In this case, those circumstances include:
the commercial
nature of James Hardie’s manufacture and distribution of asbestos-cement
the extensive
and widespread use of those products in Australia’s post-war residential
construction;
the continual
operations of James Hardie in manufacturing and distributing asbestos free
materials after 1987;
the large number
of people, tradespersons and home handypersons who would come to work on those
products over a long period of time;
the lack of any
direct commercial relationship between those tradespersons and handypersons in
respect of the asbestos-cement products
on which they worked; and
the adverse
effect on James Hardie’s business were it to warn that people who worked
on asbestos-cement products might contract
mesothelioma.
