submission (a) is correct in that it was accepted that a campaign would only
affect a small proportion of the population, the
issue is whether it would have
affected Mr Miller. I accept submission (b) in relation to a private media
campaign. It is unlikely
that Mr Miller would have responded to a
newspaper-based campaign given his media habits. I do not accept submissions
(c), (d) and
(e) for the following reasons.
evidence of Ms Caro and Mr Roberts about James Hardie making a public health
announcement in the 1980s is persuasive. Asbestos
was taken out of James Hardie
building products in about 1985. I am satisfied that an announcement and a
campaign of the type suggested
by Ms Caro would have influenced and changed Mr
Miller’s behaviour. A manufacturer of a product that warns their product
dangerous is out of the ordinary and likely to elicit attention. The product
is not unhealthy food. It is a toxic and potentially
lethal substance. Mr Miller
was a fit non-smoker who paid attention to his health and wellbeing. I find that
he would have responded
to such a campaign. The finding answers submission (d)
because an essential ingredient of any such campaign would be to identify
Hardie products. Finally, while Mr Miller’s evidence that he would only
have accepted jobs that were guaranteed to not
involve exposure to asbestos may
sound unrealistic as counsel for Amaca suggested, if a frank health warning had
been given, I am
satisfied that Mr Miller would have altered his behaviour.
There were other work options available to him, such as him moving into
commercial project electrical work as he did.
respectfully agree with the Full Court in
that a manufacturer that
advised the public of a serious public health risk with its product in a frank
and candid way would deliver
a message that most consumers would take
The submissions at trial and in this Court often treated this allegation of
breach as a failure to engage in a mass media advertising
campaign. That
description unduly focussed attention on only one element of the postulated
campaign. Properly formulated, the duty
is to warn those members of the public
engaging in the remodelling, repairing or removing of domestic
asbestos‑cement home
building products of the risk in doing so.
The most obvious first step in discharging the duty to warn is a frank public
acknowledgement by James Hardie itself of the risk,
that even occasional work on
asbestos-cement building products can cause mesothelioma. The labels it placed
on some asbestos-cement
products were hedged with qualifications which
diminished the danger. The labels were also a relatively limited publication.
same holds for the warnings in the promotional material referred to in
paragraph [307]-[311] below.
The sobering effect on the Australian community of an unequivocal and very
public warning that an activity might cause cancer should
not be underestimated.
Such an acknowledgement by James Hardie, and an endorsement by it of the
precautions which occasional users
should take, would have been inherently
newsworthy and was likely to have been published and republished by the
mainstream news media,
by government and non-government agencies, by trades
associations, building hardware suppliers and by public health and occupational
safety authorities. Moreover, in the absence of a comprehensive acknowledgement
by James Hardie of those risks, the commentary of
others naturally enough was,
as we shall see, guarded.

Klebe is a pathologist with over 18 years professional and academic experience
in asbestos-related disorders, especially
in the diagnosis and pathogenesis of
lung and malignant mesothelioma.

Professor Klebe has examined
more than a thousand cases of mesothelioma. She was
the Director of Research of the Asbestos Disease Research Institute in 2002 and
is a member of multiple international panels on mesothelioma and lung
Klebe is the author of several publications on malignant mesothelioma, including
the current international diagnostic guidelines
for diagnosis of mesothelioma.
She has published approximately 150 internationally peer-reviewed articles, a
couple of hundred abstracts
and six or seven chapters of
Klebe considers that Miller suffers from an undisputed peritoneal mesothelioma
arising from the serosal membranes lining
the chest and abdominal wall
internally. She stated that all types of asbestos are known carcinogens for
mesothelioma. She stated
that no lower threshold level of exposure has been
identified for mesothelioma for exposure to any type of asbestos.
Klebe considers that Mr Miller’s exposure at the RAH is in excess of any
background exposure from the general environment.
Exposure to asbestos above
general environment levels increases the risk of mesothelioma.
glass slides analysed by SA Pathology contain fibrous connective and adipose
tissue, as well as diaphragmatic muscle which are
all extensively infiltrated by
a malignant predominantly epithelioid tumour, with smaller focal malignant
spindle cell components.
The tumour is infiltrative and malignant, with
papillary, tubular and acinar components. It has a solid architecture. The
mesothelial
nature of the cells is affirmed by the immunohistochemical profile.
The lack of Claudin-4 markers indicate that it is an epithelioid
tumour. The
tumour demonstrates extensive outright invasive malignant features with tissue
invasion. It has features of a mesothelioma
biphasic type.
of Mr Miller’s malignant mesothelioma
to asbestos has a strong association with mesothelioma development. A study by
Wagner et al.
identified a
correlation between asbestos exposure, particularly crocidolite, and the
development of pleural mesothelioma.
asbestos fibres are inhaled, a proportion of them are deposited in lung tissue.
Highly reactive free radicals, particular oxygen
and nitrogen species, are
generated from the interaction of asbestos fibres with macrophage (white blood)
cells attempting to remove
the fibre from the lungs. The free radicals cause
damage to DNA impacting growth regulation, cell death, motility or apoptosis
death). These cells grow faster and result in abnormal cancerous
exposure results in more asbestos fibre being deposited, more fibre-macrophage
interactions and increasing radial generation
perpetuating the damage and
subsequent proliferation of mesothelial cells.
Peto Model indicates that mesothelioma can be related to cumulative asbestos
exposure. This model assesses the intensity, frequency
and duration of exposure
over time indicating the important role of time.
Miller first started working with asbestos in 1981 and was diagnosed in 2024,
resulting in a latency period of 43 years. The most
conservative and widely
accepted minimum latency of 10 years is fulfilled.
cutting, drilling, sanding and disrupting asbestos containing (AC) building
materials causes elevated concentrations of
airborne respirable asbestos fibres.
The mode used to cut the asbestos results in different release of amphibole
asbestos fibres
in comparison to chrysotile fibres. Professor Klebe states that
the concentration of crocidolite in the airborne dust created by
working with AC
materials was approximately double the proportion of crocidolite in the product
as manufactured. Sweeping fibres
with a dustpan and broom produces high peak
airborne respirable fibre concentrations.
Asbestos-containing
dust, once introduced into a workplace can persist because it is difficult to
remove the fibres entirely, even
by vacuuming. Dust can be re-suspended by
everyday activities. This means that exposure can occur beyond the time when the
led to the dust occurred.
switchboards that Mr Miller worked on likely contained Bakelite which was known
to contain up to 5% amosite (brown asbestos).
The carcinogenic risks of Bakelite
are well documented.
spaces that Miller worked in at the RAH, particularly the ceiling spaces and
catacombs, were confined and therefore any asbestos
introduced into the space
would largely have remained there regardless of Mr Miller’s own work in

no-threshold model for mesothelioma applies to both peritoneal and plural

is an identified dose-response trend for low exposure where an increase in
asbestos exposure resulted in an increase in mesothelioma
rates at approximately
47 per 100,000 person-years for pleural mesothelioma and 21 per 100,000
person-years for peritoneal mesothelioma.
and Darnton estimate that the risk of pleural mesothelioma has a convex
dose-response line that is steep for low-dose exposures
and less steep for
higher-dose exposures.
study affirms that the no-threshold model for mesothelioma risk is
appropriate.

The significance of
multiple exposures
is possible to develop mesothelioma as a result of low-dose exposure or after
exposure to high fibre loads but once the lesion
has occurred, all the fibres
that have reached the target tissue have contributed to the disease
risk of the mesothelioma-inducing chain of mutations might be higher if there
are multiple exposure events, however Professor
Klebe considers that the risk
has materialised in the form of the event itself, which is the tumour Mr Miller
Klebe expanded on her previous explanation of how asbestos exposure can lead to
the development of mesothelioma. She stated
that each exposure may cause a cell
mutation that causes a cell to grow and divide at a more rapid rate. Each time
the cell divides,
it creates daughter cells. Each daughter cell contains the
same mutation as it is a clone of the original mutated cell. This means
each clone also divides at a more rapid rate. If further contact with asbestos
fibres occurs, further mutations may lead to
further growth advantages, leading
to a local tumour growth.
Klebe suggested that it is far more likely that mesothelioma develops from
“many small mutations in tumour cells that
eventually lead to a cumulative
growth advantage, rather than a single mutation (so called driver mutation) that
single-handedly
drives tumour
The actual tumour mass then consists of genetically diverse cells that have
accumulated many small abnormalities, through multiple
molecular events, and
that continue to accumulate further mutations as the tumour evolves.
discussing the cumulative exposure model, Professor Klebe stated that
“whilst it is impossible to pinpoint
of the fibres were
responsible for what mutation at what time, it is equally impossible to dismiss
any of the fibres- the resultant
tumour is the result of a particular series of
events which may not have eventuated in this format if there was another type of
Klebe considers that in Miller’s case, like all mesothelioma cases,
“all exposures contribute” to the
development of mesothelioma, not
just the earliest
Klebe concluded that Mr Miller’s exposure to asbestos cement materials
coupled with his asbestos exposure at the RAH
during employment has
“resulted in above background exposure and falls within he most widely
accepted latency period of 10
years or more, thereby making a material
contribution to the development of peritoneal mesothelioma in Mr

Klebe explained that the mesothelium lines several body cavities including the
lungs or pleura and the peritoneum. Asbestos
fibres can be transported through
lymphatic channels from the lungs to the mesothelial lining on the surface and
cannot be coughed
out once they move into the mesothelium.
biopersistence of amphibole asbestos is more likely to cause DNA damage and the
promotion of proliferative mesotheliomal cells.
Amosite and crocidolite are in
the amphibole group. When cells interact with amphibole asbestos, many of them
die and therefore do
not develop cancer. If the effect of the asbestos persists
and enough of it stays in the tissue, there is a higher likelihood of
cancer in the long run.
Klebe agreed that Mr Miller working around pure crocidolite asbestos was at the
“right at the top of the tree in terms
of dangerous
activities”.
2R6, a British 2012 Journal of Cancer article that followed up malignant

was shown to

understood that the overall rate of mesothelioma

Klebe said that peritoneal mesothelioma is much rarer than pleural mesothelioma.
The exact rate of each varies with difference
cohorts. The most recent
comparison in the Australian Mesothelioma Register has about 40 peritoneal
mesothelioma cases and about

- 5 September 2024
Vy Broadbridge is a Medical Oncologist with experience in general malignancy,
including mesothelioma. She is Mr Miller’s
treating oncologist and is
currently a consultant oncologist at Adelaide Oncology and Haematology. She
practices at Calvary North
Adelaide, the Western Hospital and Calvary Central
Districts and is a permanent consultant oncologist at the QEH. Dr Broadbridge
received an MBBS in 2002 and has specialised in medical oncology from October
2012. She is a member of Medical Oncology Group of
Australia, the American
Society of Clinical Oncology, and the European Society of Medical Oncology.
Broadbridge first consulted Mr Miller on 22 July 2024. Two consultations
followed in August 2024. Mr Miller presented at hospital
in May with abdominal
distension, associated with bloating and loss of appetite. He had ascites along
with extensive peritoneal thickening.
A biopsy confirmed that he had peritoneal
mesothelioma. He was referred to Dr Nigel De Silva, a colorectal surgeon at the

Miller’s pathology confirmed diffuse epithelioid peritoneal malignant
mesothelioma with spindle cell component, which could
be interpreted as a
biphasic mesothelioma with sarcomatoid/desmoplastic and epithelioid
components.
Dr Broadbridge met Mr Miller on 22 July 2024, four weeks post HIPEC, he was
recovering well, with no abdominal pain, good appetite
and normal bowel
function. Mr Miller had no abnormalities in his lungs or heart. Dr Broadbridge
noted a large scar on the abdomen
from the recent surgery, which did not look
inflamed, but was otherwise soft and
non-tender.
Broadbridge discussed the rarity of the diagnosis with Mr Miller, noting that
peritoneal mesothelioma was so rare that the treatment
was extrapolated from
studies based on pleural mesothelioma. Mr Miller’s last CT scan was prior
to surgery If the next CT scan
showed no measurable disease, the management plan
would be one of ongoing monitoring. Dr Broadbridge confirmed there was no
for adjuvant therapy, given a lack of published evidence, and that if Mr
Miller developed cancer recurrence based on symptoms of
abdominal distension or
future scans, he would need to undergo doublet immunotherapies with ipilimumab
and nivolumab.

Broadbridge determined Mr Miller’s prognosis was poor, with overall
survival ranging between 9-17 months post diagnosis.
Dr Broadbridge noted
however that if patients were able to undergo HIPEC, there was marked
improvement in median survival from 34-100
months, with five year survival rates
from 30-60%. Survival was dependent on stage and histology, with sarcomatoid and
biphasic histologic
subtypes having worse outcomes compared with epithelioid
mesothelioma. Other prognostic factors included being over 75 years old,
performance status, elevated lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) and hematologic
abnormalities.
Broadbridge noted that Mr Miller was young, had good performance status, normal
LDH, no hematologic abnormalities and was able
to have HIPEC, however he has the
poorer histologic type.
Broadbridge noted that with regards to causation, it was generally accepted that
exposure to asbestos is the primary cause of malignant
mesothelioma.

Mr Miller’s employment at the
RAH and his exposure to asbestos cement
sheets, made a contribution to his
Broadbridge made the assumption that Mr Miller would progress with local and

with it likely that he would
accept recommended treatments, including immunotherapies offered, for a duration
of 24 months.

Associate Professor Ken Pittman
Professor Kenneth Pittman is a medical oncologist who provided a medical report
dated 3 December 2024 for Mr Miller.
Pittman diagnosed Mr Miller as having biphasic malignant peritoneal
mesothelioma. The predominant feature is diffuse epithelioid
mesothelioma of
tubulopapillary type, with some areas of sarcomatoid desmoplastic type.
Pittman considered that Mr Miller’s domestic and significant work exposure
to asbestos has made significant contribution
to the development of his
mesothelioma. His opinion is based upon various papers that describe an
association between asbestos exposure
and mesothelioma. He wrote that peritoneal
mesothelioma is rarer than pleural mesothelioma. Consequently, less definitive
have been published regarding the correlation between asbestos exposure
and peritoneal mesothelioma. However, in the studies that
have been published,
there appears to be a strong correlation between the two things, particularly in
relation to prognosis and life expectancy, relevant factors include age, sex,
ability to undergo successful debulking surgery and
HIPEC, residual malignancy,
and underlying pathology of the disease. Mr Miller has good prognosis factors
including his relatively
young age and having undergone HIPEC. The poor
prognostic factors include being male, the extent of the disease at time of
and the mesothelioma being biphasic (with sarcomatoid components). AP
Pittman concurred with Dr Broadbridge’s report dated
8 November 2024 which
stated that Mr Miller has a high probability of recurrent disease over the next
12 months. If there was a recurrence,
systemic therapy, including immunotherapy,
would be required. Those treatments are not considered curative. It was unclear
Pittman whether the peritoneal thickening in the CT scan in October 2024

available include chemotherapy and immunotherapy. The median time of remission
achievable with immunotherapy is eight to

Chemotherapy
inhibits processes which allow cells to divide. Immunotherapy makes the immune
cells more aggressive in the hopes that
it will cause the cells to destroy
malignant cells.
effects of immunotherapy are relatively rare but can be long lasting and
problematic.
effects of immunotherapy with mesothelioma include making cells aggressive
against tissue, affecting the skin, joints, gut, lungs
and endocrine
effects associated of chemotherapy include nausea, fatigue, hair loss,
peripheral neuropathies and weaker immune system leading
to anaemia and fatigue,
low platelet counts, increasing the risk of bruising or bleeding and
immunosuppression. A patient then has
a greater risk of
Mesothelioma
tends to be a locoregional problem. It spreads to organs in the body such as the
lungs, brain and bones. Peritonela mesothelioma
predominantly involves the
abdomen and pelvis, increasing abdominal distension and discomfort requiring
drainage of peritoneal fluid
or ascites. It can inhibit gut function and cause
with mesothelioma become weak, are in pain and require a lot of analgesia making
them drowsy and susceptible to infections
such as pneumonia. That is the usual
final mode of death.
symptoms progress and the patient becomes more debilitated, there is a need for
nursing care.
a patient dies in a hospice or at home depends on the amount of support they
have from family and others who are able to help.
Expert nursing care is
required. Those who are managed at home will usually have home nursing on a
regular basis. Early on it may
be once or twice a week and then in the terminal
stage, fairly
continuous.

Miller commenced his submissions on causation in this
Mr Miller submits that his peritoneal mesothelioma was caused or materially
contributed to by each of his multiple episodes of exposure
to asbestos during
his employment at the RAH, and asbestos cement building products manufactured
The presumption under
Dust Diseases Act 2005
(SA) operates.
Pursuant to this section, if it is proved or admitted in a dust disease action
that the plaintiff suffers from a dust
disease (as is the case here), where the
plaintiff has suffered exposure to asbestos dust which might have contributed to
the disease,
causation will be presumed. Clearly, Mr Miller has suffered
exposure to asbestos which might have contributed to this mesothelioma.
evidence has been called by the State or Amaca which would be capable of
rebutting the presumption and establishing that his exposure
to asbestos at the
RAH and from products manufactured by Amaca might not have contributed to his
prove causation, it is sufficient for Mr Miller to establish that his injuries
were “caused or materially contributed to”
by his exposure to
asbestos at the RAH and from Amaca’s
the alternative to its primary position that Mr Miller was not exposed to
asbestos at the RAH, the State submitted that the assumptions
Professor Klebe has relied upon have not been proven. The findings already
made answer that submission. The State also challenged
the basis for the
assumptions Professor Klebe’s opinion relied
...Klebe was asked by Miller’s counsel whether her opinion would be
different if she assumed that the exposure history she
was asked to accept in
preparation of her report was
as it existed, or as detailed in
Miller’s affidavit of evidence, but rather based on a new hypothetical
exposure limited ‘
to a single occasion
’ where Miller was
in the ceiling space’
clumps of fluffy fibres
fell on him’
while he worked in that space ‘
for a few hours
Over objection by the State, Klebe was permitted to answer that question and
said it did not change her opinion.
While her opinion remained unchanged, she was
whether any
exposure at the RAH, regardless of how trivial, would be sufficient to the
development of his condition. She was merely
asked about a single occasion, in a
ceiling space, with Miller being in direct contact with asbestos fibres for at
least an hour.
That is the assumption that is required to be proven by the
applicant for her (sic) Klebe’s opinion based on the new assumption
admissible. It has not been.
Moreover, it is important to appreciate that when Klebe articulates a view
concerning asbestos being a carcinogen for the development
of mesothelioma, she
is referring to respirable asbestos. This is based on the theory that the
biological mechanism commences, of
necessity, with the inhalation of asbestos
fibres. Her report makes that clear.
It follows that the mere visual presence of asbestos in one’s proximity is
not to the point. The meaning of exposure necessary
for attribution to
mesothelioma demands the inhalation of asbestos fibres. Accordingly,
interrogating the evidence of exposure requires
affirmation that Miller was
exposed to respirable asbestos fibres that were not only capable of being
inhaled, but on balance were
inhaled by him. In other words, simply being in a
space where asbestos may be present does not equate to relevant exposure. Even
being in a space where asbestos may be disturbed does not necessarily equate to
relevant exposure. It depends on whether one is in
sufficient proximity to
inhale airborne fibres for them to be inhaled.
If Miller’s evidence of exposure is rejected, it follows that the Court
cannot be asked to accept a different version of that
evidence. The only version
of his exposure is the version about which he gave evidence. A rejection of
Miller’s evidence must
inevitably lead to the conclusion that he has been
unsuccessful in proving causation.
work performed by Mr Miller at the RAH disturbed clumps of blue asbestos that
had fallen from structural steel beams and had landed
in various places,
including on the upper side of ceiling tiles, into risers and onto extruded
objects in risers and onto the floor
of the catacombs. In lifting a ceiling
tile, running cable through a ceiling space or riser, or walking through the
catacombs, crocidolite
fibres were disturbed and made airborne. The friable
nature of crocidolite and the very small size of crocidolite fibres makes it
likely that in doing the work that Mr Miller did, he inhaled airborne
crocidolite fibres. Professor Klebe’s evidence was to
that effect and I
accept it. When the scenario of Mr Miller being exposed to disturbed crocidolite
for an hour or so was put to Professor
Klebe, she said that was enough to cause
his illness. Contrary to the State’s submission, her answer was consistent
view expressed in her report that there is no recognised threshold
level of exposure to asbestos to cause mesothelioma. I find that
Miller’s level of exposure to asbestos at the RAH was materially greater
than the everyday level of exposure to asbestos
fibres that most people have. As
Professor Klebe said, exposure to crocidolite is “right at the top of the
tree in terms of
activities”.

General damages
Miller’s submissions on general damages commence in this
The task of determining an appropriate sum for general damages involves a
consideration of what has happened to Mr Miller in the
past, and what will
happen to him in the future and then fixing a figure "...
by the use of the
awareness produced by general experience, current general ideas of fairness and
", it being "
a matter of judgment in the sound exercise of
sense of proportion.
Brennan –v- James Hardie & Coy Pty Limited
2 March 2001), Curtis J said of the assessment of damages in a mesothelioma
What sum is reasonable and proportionate not only for the exquisite
pain confronting the plaintiff, the appalling reality of the manner
impending death, but also the grief and outrage at the fact of that death
itself? The sum must reflect the fact that his life
is no trifling thing. The
plaintiff must have the consolation that the community through this
values his life
for Mr Miller submitted that the award of general damages should be greater than
the award in any other mesothelioma case
in this State. Counsel referred to what
the Full Court said in

been considered anomalous for at least two
they involve the provision of compensation in circumstances where there is no

Sullivan v Gordon damages –
Dust Diseases
J considered s 9(3) of the DD Act and said
... As the text of
makes clear, what is compensable is the loss or impairment of the
plaintiff’s capacity to perform domestic services for another
Damages are not awarded pursuant to
the plaintiff’s incapacity to meet his own need for domestic services. The
award depends on the loss or reduction of the
plaintiff’s capacity to
provide services of a particular kind, namely domestic services, to another
person. The cross-appellant
submits that the words of
should be construed to give the fullest relief which the fair
meaning would allow. “[A]nother person” could be anyone
words are given their widest amplitude. I do not consider that to be the correct
construction of those words as used in
Those words take their meaning from the concept of
“domestic services”. Those domestic services must be services
or which would have been rendered to a member of the plaintiff’s
household, i.e. a person residing in the plaintiff’s
the concept of domestic services informs the qualification of the person or
persons who are or would be the recipient
of those services.
While the Note does not form part of the Act it is nonetheless available, like a
Second Reading Speech, as an extrinsic aid to interpretation.
It can be seen
that the term “domestic” imposes some limit on the nature of the
services which are compensable. In addition,
it also imposes some limit on the
identity of the other person referred to in the subsection. The award of damages
pursuant to s
9(3) is confined to compensating any loss or impairment of the
injured person’s capacity to perform such services for a member
plaintiff’s household. It does not extend to compensating for the loss or
impairment of the injured person’s capacity
to perform such services to
persons outside his or her household.
To the extent that there is any ambiguity in the phrase “for another
person” so as to suggest that the liability for
damages pursuant to
s 9(3) extends to any loss or impairment of the plaintiff’s capacity
to perform such services for any other
person beyond the plaintiff’s
household, recourse can be had to the Second Reading Speech and the Note to the
subsection.
Consideration of the second reading speech reveals that the mischief towards
which s 9(3) is directed is to ensure that care would
continue to be provided to
dependent children and spouses of persons injured by exposure to a dust
The Note provides that the subsection is intended to restore the effect
Sullivan v Gordon
. The context in which s 9(3) was enacted was
the High Court’s decision in
which overruled
the decision in
Sullivan v Gordon
Sullivan v Gordon
held that a plaintiff who is rendered unable to
provide gratuitous personal or domestic services for another because of personal
is entitled to recover as damages an amount calculated by reference to
the commercial value of those services. The New South Wales
Court of Appeal
allowed the recovery of damages for the loss of the capacity of the plaintiff to
care for her daughters.
I accept that the compensable loss for
Sullivan v Gordon
damages were, and are to be awarded
pursuant to s 9(3), remains the loss to the injured person and is not the loss
to the person
to whom the services were and are to be provided. Nonetheless,
that loss falls to be assessed in damages on the basis of the commercial
replacing those services. (footnotes omitted)
parties have very different views about this head of damage. Mr Miller claims
$293,942.33 under s 9(3) of the DD Act while the
State maintains there is no
evidence that Mr Miller is no longer able to provide the services previously
provided by him and no award
should be made.
adopt the following principles enunciated about this head of damage by the Full
An award must
commence with evidence about what an applicant formerly did and can no longer
The assessment
of the “market cost” of replacing those services must recognise that
Mr Miller claims no particular skill
or expertise in performing the
services, and any postulated reasonable market cost or commercial rates must
start with award rates
rather than agency
Calculations
should not be based on an assessment of what services should have been provided
according to Ms Dolling, but must be
based upon Mr Miller’s evidence of
what services that he previously provided to Ms
Regard must be
had to how a claimant has assisted or is able to assist in the years following
the onset of the injury or
the significant amount claimed, the hours of assistance relied upon are not
great. Mr Miller claims 2 hours per week for cleaning
tasks, 4.5 hours per
week for shopping, meal preparation and cleaning up after meals and 1 hour per
week for home maintenance for
cleaning glass, maintaining the Coffin Bay
property and maintaining cars and boats. The amount claimed is large because the
is advanced until Mr Miller’s 80
birthday in the 17
‘lost years’ (from 2027 to 2044), with a multiplier of 697 and a 3%
Mr Miller’s evidence that he does not enjoy shopping, and given the
absence of evidence from Ms England on the subject,
I do not accept the claim
advanced for food shopping. The evidence does not really assist with determining
how much time Mr Miller
and Ms England each spent preparing food per week, let
alone with the time spent shopping and cleaning up after meals. In saying
acknowledge that many couples who share the performance of domestic duties
probably never stop to measure or record these
things, but evidence is needed to
make an award.
excluding the time claimed for shopping and maintaining the Coffin Bay
property, which the evidence does not establish that
Ms England benefitted
from, I allow 5 hours per week for this head of damage. The claim is not
advanced for the past, but for after
Mr Miller passes away. The $25 hourly rate
used for non-skilled gratuitous services is appropriate to use because no
particular skill
or expertise is required to perform the tasks in question. That
produces an amount of $87,125 prior to discount and an award of $74,056.25
the 15% discount that Mr Miller suggested was appropriate.

Alex Wallace
Wallace is an accountant and a director of Forensis Accounting. He holds an MBA,

Expert Report – 18 October 2024
Wallace proceeded on the following assumptions about the claim:
Mr Miller first

Mr Miller will

of his business until
Miller’s ‘essential personal maintenance’ is estimated at $300
No deduction is
made for contingencies/vicissitudes.
Wallace noted the following about the report of Mr Thompson:
Miller’s level of work in his business has reduced significantly, although
he was providing key management services;
increased Mr Bourke’s pay to reflect his increased responsibilities and
will also pay an increased level of bonus
until the sale of the business;
Mr Miller will
cease working on 30 November 2024;
On 1 July 2025,
Mr Miller will dispose of the business and invest proceeds in an alternate
commercial investment.
addressing Mr Thompson’s report, Mr Wallace stated:
Mr Thompson used
the ‘lost profit approach’ discussed at paras [14.5] and [14.17] of
his report;
The lost profit
approach is said by Mr Thompson to be applicable if it is established if the
business cannot run without Mr Miller;
Mr Thompson does
not directly answer that question, however based on para. [14.4(iii)], it
appears that Mr Thompson considers that
an answer cannot be given until after Mr
Miller is replaced as the owner/operator of the business;
There is no
logical basis for concluding that ‘the most factually appropriate’
measure of loss is the lost profit approach
and it is more appropriate/logical
to assume that the business can continue to operate without Mr Miller;
It follows that
Mr Wallace considers that the replacement labour approach ought to be used
rather than the lost profit approach.
Thompson compared the alternate return on capital to lost profits (paras [14.5
(iii)] and [23.1]). In Mr Wallace’s opinion,
when assessing economic loss
caused by personal injury, it is wrong to compare profits to returns on capital
as they are different
things. The loss of profits has been directly caused by
the injury whereas the gain that comprises a return on capital investment
not been directly caused by the injury.
Mr Wallace’s opinion, if indirect losses and gains are included in a
personal-injury economic loss calculation, other indirect
gains and losses would
also need to be considered. Based on the request that he calculate loss suffered
by Miller as a result of
his illness, Mr Wallace believes that returns on
capital should not be included in the analysis. Additionally, calculating
on capital requires a forecast on expected investment returns, which
unavoidably leads to forecasting inflation. Mr Wallace referred
to a passage
Assessment of Damages for Personal Injury and
which states
“that in an action for damages for personal injury, evidence as to the
likely course of inflation ... is
inadmissible”.
Wallace believes that Mr Thompson’s calculation of true profit is subject
to the following issues:
subsidies have been excluded from the true profit;
Profit from the
sale of non-current assets of $16,998 for the year 2020 have not been excluded
which conflicts with Mr Thompson’s
statement that he has deducted
‘Profit on the Sale of Assets’ (para. [13.2(v)(b)]);
Mr Thompson has
adjusted his calculations of true profit by the movement in CPI (para.
[13.2(vi)]), despite not establishing that
the business has historically been
able to raise its prices at the same rate as the movement in the
Thompson has calculated the business profit but for the illness at $390,000 per
annum before tax, on the arithmetic average of
his calculation of true profit
for the 2022 to 2024 years (para. [21.1]). Due to the issues mentioned, Mr
Wallace has calculated
this amount to $376,500, where the profits are not
adjusted by the movement in CPI.
Wallace considers that it is inappropriate to rely on Mr Thompson’s
‘indicative valuation’ of the business as
Mr Thompson has not
supported the key valuation assumptions that he has adopted with empirical
evidence. For example, Mr Wallace
expects that capitalisation rates should be
based on capitalisation rates used in comparable transactions instead of a
capitalisation
rate of 50% based on Mr Thompson’s ‘general
experience’ and ‘understanding of similar
transactions’.

the value of the Business.

to assume that
Mr Bourke’s bonus will double.

to maintenance.

Exemplary damages
Miller claims exemplary damages from the State and Amaca. Section 9(2) of
the DD Act provides:
The District Court or SAET (as the case may be) should make an award of
exemplary damages in each case against a defendant if it
is satisfied that the
(a) knew that the injured person was at risk of exposure to asbestos dust, or
carried on a prescribed industrial or commercial process
that resulted in the
injured person's exposure to asbestos dust; and
(b) knew, at the time of the injured person's exposure to asbestos dust, that
exposure to asbestos dust could result in a dust disease.
Miller contends that both those requirements are satisfied in relation to the
State and Amaca. While he contends that each respondent
knew that exposure to
asbestos dust could result in a dust disease, if it be necessary, Mr Miller also
relies on the deeming provision
in s 8(2) of the DD Act, which provides that the
onus to prove that a party did not know of the danger of asbestos rests with the
party. Mr Miller referred to
BHP Billiton Limited v
Where Doyle CJ
and White J said the
...We consider that the effect of s 9(2) is to establish conditions which are
sufficient for an award of exemplary damages in a dust
diseases action, and to
indicate that if those circumstances exist, an award should usually be made.
That is to say, if the defendant
is in the category identified in sub-par (a)
and had the knowledge to which sub-par (b) refers, the Court ought, absent some
countervailing consideration, make an award of exemplary damages. It
is no longer necessary that a defendant’s conduct be characterised
reprehensible before an award is made (although if a defendant’s conduct
is reprehensible, that would be relevant to both
the exercise of the discretion
and to the assessment of the amount to be awarded). Section 9(2) should be
understood as substituting
the statutory criteria for the common law criteria;
not as adding to those criteria. There will be circumstances in which, despite
the use of the word “should”, the Court may exercise the discretion
not to make an award, but those circumstances will
be outside the norm.
This has the effect that an award of exemplary damages may be made against a
defendant in respect of a breach of duty occurring many
years previously, who no
longer has any involvement with asbestos, whose conduct does not in the eye of
the common law warrant punishment,
and in respect of whom notions of deterrence
are of no or limited weight. That however is the legislative intention. There is
indication that s 9(2) is not to apply in such circumstances and, indeed,
they are the circumstances which are likely to exist in
many, if not most, dust
diseases actions.
It should be kept in mind that many dust diseases do not become manifest for
many years after the injured person’s exposure
to asbestos. The evidence
in this action indicated that periods of 15 to 30 years are common. In these
circumstances it is almost
inevitable that some defendants will have ceased
business altogether, or changed their practices, or ceased any use of asbestos.
Nevertheless, s 9(2) contemplates that an award of exemplary damages should be
Miller submitted that at first instance in
, a finding was made on
evidence identical to the evidence in this case, that s 9(2) of the DD Act was
satisfied. The award of exemplary
damages was $30,000 at first instance. It was
increased to $250,000 on appeal. The appellate court found that Amaca’s
was both egregious and
reprehensible.
also referred to paras [636] to [652] of
where the principles that apply to exemplary damages are discussed. The
first observation the Full Court made in
At common law, exemplary damages are awarded against a wrongdoer who is guilty
of reprehensible conduct representing, as it is often
“contumelious disregard of another’s
re-assessing exemplary damages, the Full Court
Where a plaintiff wishes to bring to account, in addition to the statutory
preconditions, any common law considerations which have
been recognised as
relevant to an award of exemplary damages, the burden remains with the plaintiff
to adduce evidence and to prove
those matters which it is said demonstrate a
basis for punishment and condemnation. Whilst, in a general sense, one may wish
and condemn James Hardie for its conduct, the award in favour of Mr
Werfel must focus on the circumstances of its negligent conduct
Werfel, or the class of which he is a member, and identify exactly what it is
of punishment and
condemnation.
That is, if a plaintiff wishes to demonstrate why at common law an award of
exemplary damages ought be made, proof must start with
the particular negligence
finding made against the defendant, and then identify what it is about that
conduct, necessarily beyond
the statutory preconditions supplied by a
combination of
Diseases Act
, which justifies an award.
Court concluded that Mr Werfel had not established that the common law criteria
to award exemplary damages had been satisfied.
Because James Hardie might again
be punished under
DD Act, the Court said that the award should
“be characterised by
moderation”.
Court awarded $35,000 for exemplary damages.
award of exemplary damages can be made even though the conduct complained of has
However, cessation
of the conduct is relevant to the quantum of the award as there is no need for
specific deterrence. The Full Court
said that general deterrence
is still relevant because there may still be businesses that handle
relevant consideration discussed in
is the effect of a prior award
of exemplary damages against a party for the same or similar conduct. In
, Stanley J suggested that prior awards might need to be taken into
account when assessing the quantum of exemplary
In that regard, I am conscious that Amaca might be exposed to further actions in
the future in which a claim for exemplary damages
could be made. This is the
first award of exemplary damages made against the cross-respondent. It is for
courts in the future who
may have to assess a claim for exemplary damages to
determine whether, if an award of such damages is to be made, it should be
because of any earlier award made against the cross-respondent for
exemplary damages for the conduct which justified the award in
Full Court in
confirmed that prior awards made against a party
should be taken into account when assessing exemplary
Plainly what the Full Court in
Amaca v Latz
had in mind is that
a relevant consideration when assessing exemplary damages is that there must be
a conclusion to punishment, and
that there may come a time when lesser awards
ought be made before punishment is complete...
on the evidence submitted by Mr Miller, I make the same five findings that were
. The findings were summarised by the Full Court in
Hardie is party to a charitable trust which it established to compensate the
victims of asbestos disease caused by that company,
and by which a fund was
established to deal with claims for which James Hardie is
Judge had documentary evidence demonstrating that James Hardie knew about the
risk to the health of its workers from asbestos
dust by 1938, and this awareness
increased over the years. By the early 1960s, James Hardie knew that asbestos
sheeting could cause
mesothelioma and that the users of those products were at
risk of developing that disease, but it made a conscious choice not to
public about that. By 1976, James Hardie knew of precautions that could be taken
to minimise the risk of contracting asbestos
related diseases as a result of
cutting asbestos products and it implemented precautions for its own
1963, a safety officer with James Hardie met with senior executives and raised
concerns about the threat posed by the company’s
products as a result of
cutting cement sheets made with asbestos. He suggested that those products be
labelled. He also suggested
that the company had a moral obligation not to give
those who used James Hardie products the impression that there was no problem
with asbestos. A senior executive and the company physician told the safety
officer that, once a product had been sold, the company
had no further
obligation.
1966, an executive of James Hardie referred to a newspaper article describing
the lethal effects of mesothelioma and was concerned
information getting around Australia”. That same year, James
Hardie’s industrial physician
addressed a conference of factor managers
and told them that exposure to asbestos was dangerous and cumulative, and that
asbestosis and cancer. For commercial reasons, James Hardie continued
to manufacture, market and sell asbestos products, including
Hardie’s conduct occurred in a context where it was fully informed about
the lethal dangers of asbestos but did not warn
its customers about the
potential harm associated with using its products, and failed to advise of the
known precautions that might
have been used to minimise the harm. Stanley J
found that this conduct “amounted to reckless
indifference”.
setting aside the award of exemplary damages at first instance in
the Full Court said that the lack of precise first instance findings was
‘particularly
important’,
was not appropriate to simply adopt an earlier award without explanation. I
consider that the findings made in this case are
sufficient to enable a more
substantial award than the award made on appeal in
. Further, and

the State was negligent in failing to take proper care for the safety and

Contribution between the State and Amaca
State observed that there is no right of contribution between tortfeasors at
common law and that the issue of contribution and
apportionment of liability is
dealt with by the
Law Reform (Contributory
Negligence and Apportionment of Liability) Act 2001
(LR Act), s 6 of which,
relevantly to this matter, provides:
Right to contribution
(1) A person who is liable in damages for harm suffered by another may recover
contribution from a third person who is also liable
in damages for the same
(2) The right to contribution—
(a) exists even though the act or omission that gave rise to the liability of
the person seeking to recover contribution may amount
to an offence; and
(b) extends to liabilities incidental to damages (such as a liability for
(but the right is subject to Part 3 and any other statutory provision that may
operate to modify, exclude or limit it in a particular
(3) An action for contribution may be brought—
(a) by way of third party proceedings, or proceedings between the parties, in an
action in which damages are sought from the person
entitled to contribution;
(b) by way of a separate action brought within the relevant time limit against
the person from whom contribution is sought.
relevant time limit
is the longer of the following—
(a) the period within which the person who suffered the harm could have brought
an action against the person from whom contribution
(b) 2 years after the damages payable by the person entitled to contribution are
finally determined.
(5) The contribution is to be an amount that is fair and equitable having regard
to the extent of each contributory's responsibility
for the harm.
(6) In determining the contribution to be made by a contributory whose liability
for the harm is a derivative liability, the court
is to have regard to the act
or omission from which the liability arose.
(7) If it is fair and equitable to do so, the court may exempt a person from
liability to make contribution or order that the contribution
to be recovered is
to amount to a complete indemnity.
(8) ... (notes omitted)
on the findings that have been made, s 6(1) of the LR Act applies to this
matter. The State and Amaca are entitled to seek contribution
from each other.
Section 6(5) of the LR Act provides that where there are multiple tortfeasors,
the extent of the contribution made
by a tortfeasor is determined by what is
fair and equitable having regard to the extent of the responsibility of each
for the harm.
The State’s submissions in relation
to contribution and apportionment
State cited
Wynbergen v Hoyts Corporation Pty
dealing with NSW legislation in similar terms as s 6 of the LR Act, Hayne J
No doubt the making of the apportionment which the legislation requires involves
comparison of the culpability of the parties, ie,
the degree to which each has
departed from the standard of what is
reasonable,
but that is
not the only element to be considered. Regard must be had to the "relative
importance of the acts of the parties in causing
conduct of each negligent party in relation to the
circumstances of the accident which must be subjected to comparative
examination"

the NSW Court
of Appeal considered the apportionment of liability between two defendants in a
mesothelioma case. James Hardie appealed
the first instance apportionment and
relied upon the decision in
E.M. Baldwin and Son
Pty Ltd v Plane
that case a majority of the NSW Court of Appeal held that the only apportionment
available was an equal one because of the nature
of the disease of
mesothelioma:
In my opinion, the evidence provides no rational basis for the division of
causation between the earlier and later periods of Mr
Plane's employment by
Baldwin, especially when regard is paid to the complex pathogenesis of
mesothelioma explained by Professor
Henderson. Axiomatically, causation
therefore cannot be proportionally allocated between his different work
activities or the different
amphibole asbestos fibres to which he was
In these circumstances, I consider it impossible to establish that any
apportionment is ‘just and equitable’ other than
apportionment of responsibility for Mr Plane's damage between Baldwin and
(citations omitted)
disagreed with that approach. Sheller JA, with whom the
rest of the Court agreed,
Judge Curtis applied the dictum of Clarke JA in
Macquarie Pathology
Service Pty Limited v Sullivan
(unreported) Court of Appeal, 28 March
1995. The appellant first submitted that, having found the defendants equally
blameworthy
and set the causal potency at 75 per cent to the appellant and 25
per cent to the second respondent, the trial Judge ignored equal
blameworthiness
and simply apportioned the responsibility for the plaintiff's damages
75 per cent to the appellant and 25 per cent
to the second respondent. On
its face what his Honour did seems entirely sensible. He regarded the parties as
equally blameworthy,
but regarded the causal potency of the appellant as greater
in a proportion of three to one to that of the second respondent. It
mathematical exercise, and where the responsibilities were not to be
distinguished in one respect but were to be distinguished
in another respect I
see no reason why the distinguishing respect should not be given full effect. To
apportion as Judge Curtis did
seems just and equitable.
Professor Henderson's evidence, which Fitzgerald AJA had earlier said the
Tribunal substantially accepted, was that while there was
uncertainty concerning
the process of development of mesothelioma it was related to the cumulative
effect of all asbestos fibre inhaled
in the material period which ends about ten
years before the first diagnosis of the disease. The Tribunal, his Honour
thought, correctly
accepted this theory and rejected the manufacturer's
fundamental proposition that inhalation of more than one amphibole form of
fibre raised a number of separate, independent, possible causes of
mesothelioma and the conclusion for which it contended, namely,
plaintiff's mesothelioma was probably caused by only one of the other amphiboles
which he had probably inhaled.
In the present case there was evidence before the Tribunal from Dr James
Leigh that for all fibre types there is a dose response
relationship between the
risk of asbestosis, lung cancer and mesothelioma. The appellant submitted,
however, that apportionment must
be calculated by reference to causal potency,
and in the absence of evidence providing a rational basis for the division of
between the defendants there could not be apportionment by reference
to the extent of risk related to the length of exposure or the
On the evidence in this case Judge Curtis found that the degree of exposure, and
hence the degree of risk, varied between the two
defendants. I can see no error
in his Honour applying the relevant proportions to determine what was a just and
equitable apportionment
having regard to each defendant's responsibility for the
E M Baldwin & Son Pty Ltd v
is properly understood as being a factual determination by this
Court of approximate equality of culpability in a case where the
entitled to re-assess that culpability, the decision is one restricted to its
particular facts and of no relevance in this
appeal. On the other hand if it is
to be regarded, as it fairly might be, as a statement of legal principle that,
in circumstances
where successive employers have exposed a worker to the risk of
injury from asbestos over different periods with, perhaps, different
intensities
of exposure to asbestos with different degrees of toxicity, apportionment of
liability between successive negligent employees,
found to have caused disease
resulting from such exposure, must be equal, then in my respectful opinion, such
statement is wrong.
In cases of this sort, where causation is proved by evidence
of exposure to risk and there is a rational basis for distinguishing
between the
degrees of exposure, it is not correct to say that there is no rational basis
for the division of causation. Nor, indeed,
should the conventional regard to
culpability and causation obscure the need to examine the whole conduct of each
party, an examination
which in circumstances of inability to quantify
comparative causal potency could itself lead to differential responsibility by
to the degree of exposure to risk.
passage answers the State’s submission that acceptance of the evidence of
Professor Klebe precludes apportioning liability
in this case based on different
causal potency exposures. Legal proof is very different to proof to a scientific
standard, as Priestly
JA observed in
State of New South Wales t/a New South
Wales Department of Agriculture v
The decision at first instance is a paradigm example of a feature of fact
finding often found in cases involving medical issues.
That feature is the major
cleavage between proof of a fact in non criminal court cases to the satisfaction
of the fact finding tribunal
on the balance of probabilities and proof of a fact
for scientific purposes to the satisfaction of those expert in the particular
field of science. The latter kind of proof is much more rigorous and demanding
than the former.
The two kinds of proof are quite different in their objects and methods, but are
frequently the cause of confusion when medical issues
are concerned. In many
such cases, experts in the field of the relevant medical science give evidence
of their expert opinion concerning
the medical issue. Trained in the scientific
method of proof, some such experts find difficulty in adjusting themselves, when
evidence in court, to the lesser requirements of legal proof, which,
looked at from their scientific standpoint, they regard as inferior
unreliable...

, the Full Court discussed the obligations of James Hardie as a
manufacturer and supplier of asbestos products. The comments have
application to
the role of BI in this
In general terms, the imposition on manufacturers and suppliers of products of a
duty to take reasonable care not to cause injury
to the consumers of their
products is uncontroversial. It is also well established that the standard of
care may extend to warning
of known dangers and to instruct on appropriate
precautions. The duty is a continuing one, such that a manufacturer or
supplier who
subsequently becomes aware of a product danger must take such
reasonably practicable measures as are available to recall or warn.
an element of the duty to take active steps to discover possible product
dangers. Moreover, it is recognised that discharge
of the duty may extend
to instructing third parties, such as health care workers, on symptoms that they
should be alert to for the
purposes of diagnosing the effects of a defective
product. (citations omitted)
continuing nature of the duty described in that extract suggests that although
BISA sprayed asbestos at the RAH 50 years or more
ago, the passage of time does
not relieve BI of the obligation to take reasonably practicable measures to
recall or issue a warning
Further, the observation that the duty may extend to warning third parties about
risk associated with a manufacturer’s product has application here. The
authority referred to in
is a 1991 product liability case decision
of the Full Court of the Supreme Court of Victoria where the Court said
The question is, "what is the scope of that duty of care?" Is it to take
reasonable care to avoid injury to the appellant from her
use or continued use
of the product? If such is the duty then in determining whether the respondents
were in breach of that duty
it is necessary to evaluate the magnitude of that
risk and all other relevant circumstances and matters in order to determine
the respondents' response to the risk was reasonable or whether it was
such as to constitute a breach of the duty owed: see
Wyong Shire Council v
, at p 47. If, however, as was contended for on behalf of the appellant
the scope of the duty was to warn the appellant as being a
member of the class
of persons to whom the duty was owed, of the risk involved in using or
continuing to use Carefree Super tampons
then, putting aside any question of
causation in the event of a breach of that duty, the appellant would be
entitled to succeed.
are reasonable measures to take to protect someone from a dangerous product will
depend on the facts and circumstances of each
The State’s
duty of care required it to take reasonable steps to protect Mr Miller,
including by warning him about the danger
of asbestos. The existence of that
duty does not relieve BI of its duty to take reasonable care to avoid injury to
Mr Miller by working
in proximity to its product. The Full Court in
observed that duty extends to warning of known dangers and instructing about
appropriate precautions. It is not necessary to adopt
the broader formulation of
the duty in the latter part of that passage for the State to recover
contribution from BI on these facts.

High Court described matters relevant to apportionment in
Podrebersek v
Australian Iron and Steel Pty
The making of an apportionment as between a plaintiff and a defendant of their
respective shares in the responsibility for the damage
involves a comparison
both of culpability, i.e. of the degree of departure from the standard of care
of the reasonable man (
Pennington v. Norris
[1956] HCA 26
(1956) 96 CLR 10
, at 16) and of the
relative importance of the acts of the parties in causing the damage:
v. Gypsum Mines Ltd
[1953] UKHL 4
(1953) AC 663
Smith v. McIntyre
[1958] TASStRp 11
[1958] Tas.S.R. 36
at 42-49 and
Broadhurst v. Millman
[1976] VicRp 15
(1976) VR 208
at 219 and cases there cited. It is the whole conduct of each negligent party in
relation to the circumstances of the accident which
must be subjected to
comparative examination. The significance of the various elements involved in
such an examination will vary
from case to case; for example, the circumstances
of some cases may be such that a comparison of the relative importance of the
of the parties in causing the damage will be of little, if any, importance.
to Mr Miller commencing to work at the RAH in 1991, both the State and BI knew

was toxic and that relatively
minor exposures to it could result in death. Evidence was not led about the

had in relation to the danger of asbestos when

relied on the Applicant’s
Comprehensive Bundle of Documents from the State of South Australia’s
Discovery, exhibit A37,
in that regard. The bundle contains Good Health
Bulletins that are general in nature and deal with a broad range of OHS and
health issues.
Health Bulletin 115 of July 1960 discusses asbestos along with silica and coal
dust under the heading ‘Inanimate
The article
states that very small particles of silica, asbestos and coal are dangerous
because they may not be cleared from the
lungs by coughing and identifies the
circumstances in which asbestos may pose a risk to
Fine particles of asbestos fibre in the form of dust may be produced in the
mining, processing, or transport of this substance.
article does not mention sprayed asbestos but focuses on the mining, processing
and transportation of asbestos. In the absence
of some other evidence, Bulletin
115 and the earlier bulletins referred to do not persuade me that the State had
the level of knowledge
when asbestos was sprayed at the RAH.
the 20 years or so after crocidolite was last sprayed at the RAH, the State and
relevant managers of the RAH learned that it
was dangerous and potentially
lethal if inhaled. The State and the RAH knew or ought to have had that
knowledge by no later than
1980. The knowledge was documented in Technical
Bulletin 24 which was published prior to 1980.
1209 The State
had 11 years or more after Technical Bulletin 24 was published to warn Mr Miller
and others like him of the danger
posed by sprayed crocidolite in the RAH.
Wearing masks in ceiling spaces, risers and the catacombs should have been
mandated by the
RAH but was not. The failure to do those things over a period of
11 years or more distinguishes this case from cases where BI has
been found to
have a greater responsibility than have other
tortfeasors.
conduct is culpable because it failed to issue any warning about the danger of
the crocidolite it sprayed at the RAH. The
State’s conduct is culpable
because it became aware of the danger of sprayed crocidolite at least 11 years
prior to Mr Miller
working at the RAH, but failed to warn him of that danger or
provide him with a mask and direct him to wear a mask whilst working
spaces, risers and the catacombs. The employer of the plaintiff in
was found to have constructive knowledge of the danger of
asbestos. Those responsible for asbestos safety in the RAH had actual knowledge
of the danger of sprayed crocidolite before Mr Miller worked there. For that
reason, and because the RAH could have used inexpensive
and easily implemented
measures to reduce the risk of injury, the State should bear more responsibility
than BI. BI is liable to
contribute to 40% of the damages payable by the State
to Mr Miller, except for the exemplary damages which are payable by the State.
Miller has proved that the State and Amaca caused him injury, loss and damage,
owed him a duty of care and breached that duty.
Mr Miller’s claim in
negligence succeeds. Damages are assessed at $2,261,927.75.
State is liable for 80% of damages and Amaca is responsible for 20% of damages.
That apportionment does not apply to the exemplary
damages payable by each
respondent.
State is entitled to contribution from BI equivalent to 40% of the damages
payable by the State to Mr Miller. That contribution
does not apply to the
exemplary damages payable by the State.
parties are to attempt to agree a form of final orders within 7 days of
delivery of these reasons failing which each party is
to submit a draft form of
final orders by the same date.
